When I got all wor out wid werk, en wid de callin’ for u, en went to sleep, mi hart wuz mos’ broek bekase u wuz los’, en I didn’ k’yer no’ mo’ whut becum er me en de raf’. En when I waek up en fien u bak agin, all saef en soun’, de teers cum, en I cuud a got doun on mi nees en kis yo’ fuut, I’s so thankful. En all u wuz thinkin’ ’bout wuz how u cuud maek a fool uv oelay Jim wid a lie. Dat truk dah is trash; en trash is whut peepl is dat puuts dert on de hed er day fren’s en maeks ’em ashaemd.”

Then he got up slo and waukt to th wigwom, and went in thair without saying enything but that. But that was enuf. It maed me feel so meen I cuud allmoest kist his fuut to get him to taek it bak.

It was fifteen minits befor I cuud werk mieself up to go and humbl mieself to a nigger; but I dun it, and I worn’t ever sorry for it afterwards, neether. I didn’t do him no mor meen triks, and I wouldn’t dun that wun if I’d a knowed it wuud maek him feel that way.












CHAPTER XVI.

We slept moest all day, and started out at niet, a litl ways behiend a monstrus long raft that was as long going bi as a proseshun. She had foer long sweeps at eech end, so we jujd she carryd as meny as therty men, liekly. She had fiev big wigwams abord, wied apart, and an oepen camp fier in th midl, and a tall flag-poel at eech end. Thair was a power of stiel about her. It amounted to sumthing being a raftsman on such a craft as that.

We went drifting doun into a big bend, and th niet clouded up and got hot. Th river was verry wied, and was walld with solid timber on boeth sieds; u couldn’t see a braek in it hardly ever, or a liet. We taukt about Cairo, and wunderd whether we wuud noe it when we got to it. I sed liekly we wouldn’t, becauz I had herd say thair worn’t but about a duzen houses thair, and if thay didn’t hapen to hav them lit up, how was we going to noe we was pasing a toun? Jim sed if th too big rivers joind together thair, that wuud sho. But I sed maybe we miet think we was pasing th fuut of an ieland and cuming into th saem oeld river agen. That disterbd Jim—and me too. So th qeschun was, whut to do? I sed, padl ashor th ferst tiem a liet shoed, and tel them pap was behiend, cuming along with a traeding-scow, and was a green hand at th biznes, and wonted to noe how far it was to Cairo. Jim thaut it was a guud iedeea, so we tuuk a smoek on it and waeted.

Thair worn’t nuthing to do now but to luuk out sharp for th toun, and not pas it without seeing it. He sed he’d be miety shur to see it, becauz he’d be a free man th minit he seen it, but if he mist it he’d be in a slaev cuntry agen and no mor sho for freedom. Evry litl whiel he jumps up and sez:

“Dah she is?”

But it worn’t. It was Jak-o’-lanterns, or lietning bugs; so he set doun agen, and went to woching, saem as befor. Jim sed it maed him all oever trembly and feeverish to be so cloez to freedom. Wel, I can tel u it maed me all oever trembly and feeverish, too, to heer him, becauz I begun to get it thru mi hed that he was moest free—and hoo was to blaem for it? Whi, me. I couldn’t get that out of mi conshens, no how nor no way. It got to trubling me so I couldn’t rest; I couldn’t stay stil in wun plaes. It hadn’t ever cum hoem to me befor, whut this thing was that I was doing. But now it did; and it stayd with me, and scorcht me mor and mor. I tried to maek out to mieself that I worn’t to blaem, becauz I didn’t run Jim off frum his rietful oener; but it worn’t no ues, conshens up and sez, evry tiem, “But u knowed he was runing for his freedom, and u cuud a paddled ashor and toeld sumbody.” That was so—I couldn’t get around that noeway. That was whair it pincht. Conshens sez to me, “Whut had pur Mis Watson dun to u that u cuud see her nigger go off riet under yur ies and never say wun singgl werd? Whut did that pur oeld wuuman do to u that u cuud treet her so meen? Whi, she tried to lern u yur buuk, she tried to lern u yur maners, she tried to be guud to u evry way she knowed how. That’s whut she dun.”

I got to feeling so meen and so mizerabl I moest wisht I was ded. I fidgeted up and doun th raft, abuezing mieself to mieself, and Jim was fidgeting up and doun past me. We neether of us cuud keep stil. Evry tiem he danst around and sez, “Dah’s Cairo!” it went thru me liek a shot, and I thaut if it was Cairo I rekond I wuud die of miserableness.

Jim taukt out loud all th tiem whiel I was tauking to mieself. He was saying how th ferst thing he wuud do when he got to a free Staet he wuud go to saeving up muny and never spend a singgl sent, and when he got enuf he wuud bi his wief, which was oend on a farm cloez to whair Mis Watson livd; and then thay wuud boeth werk to bi th too children, and if thair master wouldn’t sel them, thay’d get an Ab’litionist to go and steel them.

It moest froez me to heer such tauk. He wouldn’t ever daird to tauk such tauk in his lief befor. Just see whut a diferens it maed in him th minit he jujd he was about free. It was acording to th oeld saying, “Giv a nigger an inch and he’ll taek an el.” Thinks I, this is whut cums of mi not thinking. Heer was this nigger, which I had as guud as helpt to run away, cuming riet out flat-fuuted and saying he wuud steel his children—children that belongd to a man I didn’t eeven noe; a man that hadn’t ever dun me no harm.

I was sorry to heer Jim say that, it was such a loeering of him. Mi conshens got to stering me up hoter than ever, until at last I sez to it, “Let up on me—it ain’t too laet yet—I’ll padl ashor at th ferst liet and tel.” I felt eezy and hapy and liet as a fether riet off. All mi trubls was gon. I went to luuking out sharp for a liet, and sort of singing to mieself. Bi and bi wun shoed. Jim sings out:

“We’s saef, Huck, we’s saef! Jump up and crak yo’ heels! Dat’s de guud oelay Cairo at las’, I jis noes it!”

I sez:

“I’ll taek th canoo and go and see, Jim. It mightn’t be, u noe.”

He jumpt and got th canoo redy, and puut his oeld coet in th botom for me to set on, and giv me th padl; and as I shuvd off, he sez:

“Pooty soon I’ll be a-shout’n’ for joy, en I’ll say, it’s all on acounts o’ Huck; I’s a free man, en I couldn’t ever ben free ef it hadn’ ben for Huck; Huck dun it. Jim wun’t ever forgit u, Huck; u’s de bes’ fren’ Jim’s ever had; en u’s de oenly fren’ oelay Jim’s got now.”

I was padling off, all in a swet to tel on him; but when he sez this, it seemd to kiend of taek th tuk all out of me. I went along slo then, and I worn’t riet doun serten whether I was glad I started or whether I worn’t. When I was fifty yards off, Jim sez:

“Dah u goes, de oelay troo Huck; de on’y whiet genlman dat ever kep’ his promis to oelay Jim.”

Wel, I just felt sik. But I sez, I got to do it—I can’t get out of it. Riet then along cums a skif with too men in it with guns, and thay stopt and I stopt. Wun of them sez:

“Whut’s that yonder?”

“A pees of a raft,” I sez.

“Do u belong on it?”

“Yes, ser.”

“Eny men on it?”

“Oenly wun, ser.”

“Wel, thair’s fiev niggers run off to-niet up yonder, abuv th hed of th bend. Is yur man whiet or blak?”

I didn’t anser up prompt. I tried to, but th werds wouldn’t cum. I tried for a second or too to braes up and out with it, but I worn’t man enuf—hadn’t th spunk of a rabit. I see I was weekening; so I just giv up trieing, and up and sez:

“He’s whiet.”

“I rekon we’ll go and see for ourselvs.”

“I wish u wuud,” sez I, “becauz it’s pap that’s thair, and maybe u’d help me toe th raft ashor whair th liet is. He’s sik—and so is mam and Mary Ann.”

“O, th devil! we’re in a hery, boy. But I s’poez we’ve got to. Cum, bukl to yur padl, and let’s get along.”

I bukld to mi padl and thay laed to thair ors. When we had maed a stroek or too, I sez:

“Pap’ll be miety much obleeged to u, I can tel u. Evrybody goes away when I wont them to help me toe th raft ashor, and I can’t do it bi mieself.”

“Wel, that’s infernal meen. Od, too. Say, boy, whut’s th mater with yur faather?”

“It’s th—a—th—wel, it ain’t enything much.”

Thay stopt puuling. It worn’t but a miety litl ways to th raft now. Wun sez:

“Boy, that’s a lie. Whut is th mater with yur pap? Anser up sqair now, and it’ll be th beter for u.”









“I wil, ser, I wil, onest—but don’t leev us, pleez. It’s th—th—Jentlmen, if u’ll oenly puul ahed, and let me heev u th hedlien, u wun’t hav to cum a-neer th raft—pleez do.”

“Set her bak, John, set her bak!” sez wun. Thay bakt wauter. “Keep away, boy—keep to looard. Confound it, I just expect th wiend has blowed it to us. Yur pap’s got th small-pox, and u noe it preshus wel. Whi didn’t u cum out and say so? Do u wont to spred it all oever?”

“Wel,” sez I, a-blubbering, “I’ve toeld evrybody befor, and thay just went away and left us.”

“Pur devil, thair’s sumthing in that. We ar riet doun sorry for u, but we—wel, hang it, we don’t wont th small-pox, u see. Luuk heer, I’ll tel u whut to do. Don’t u tri to land bi yurself, or u’ll smash evrything to peeses. U floet along doun about twenty miels, and u’ll cum to a toun on th left-hand sied of th river. It wil be long after sun-up then, and when u ask for help u tel them yur foeks ar all doun with chils and feever. Don’t be a fool agen, and let peepl ges whut is th mater. Now we’re trieing to do u a kiendnes; so u just puut twenty miels between us, that’s a guud boy. It wouldn’t do eny guud to land yonder whair th liet is—it’s oenly a wuud-yard. Say, I rekon yur faather’s pur, and I’m bound to say he’s in prity hard luk. Heer, I’ll puut a twenty-dolar goeld pees on this bord, and u get it when it floets bi. I feel miety meen to leev u; but mi kingdom! it wun’t do to fool with small-pox, don’t u see?”

“Hoeld on, Parker,” sez th uther man, “heer’s a twenty to puut on th bord for me. Guud-bi, boy; u do as Mr. Parker toeld u, and u’ll be all riet.”

“That’s so, mi boy—guud-bi, guud-bi. If u see eny runaway niggers u get help and nab them, and u can maek sum muny bi it.”

“Guud-bi, ser,” sez I; “I wun’t let no runaway niggers get bi me if I can help it.”

Thay went off and I got abord th raft, feeling bad and lo, becauz I knowed verry wel I had dun rong, and I see it worn’t no ues for me to tri to lern to do riet; a body that don’t get started riet when he’s litl ain’t got no sho—when th pinch cums thair ain’t nuthing to bak him up and keep him to his werk, and so he gets beet. Then I thaut a minit, and sez to mieself, hoeld on; s’poez u’d a dun riet and giv Jim up, wuud u felt beter than whut u do now? No, sez I, I’d feel bad—I’d feel just th saem way I do now. Wel, then, sez I, whut’s th ues u lerning to do riet when it’s trublsum to do riet and ain’t no trubl to do rong, and th waejes is just th saem? I was stuk. I couldn’t anser that. So I rekond I wouldn’t bother no mor about it, but after this allways do whichever cum handyest at th tiem.

I went into th wigwom; Jim worn’t thair. I luukt all around; he worn’t enywhair. I sez:

“Jim!”

“Heer I is, Huck. Is day out o’ siet yit? Don’t tauk loud.”









He was in th river under th stern or, with just his noez out. I toeld him thay wer out of siet, so he cum abord. He sez:

“I was a-listenin’ to all de tauk, en I slips into de river en was gwyne to shuv for sho’ if day cum abord. Den I was gwyne to swim to de raf’ agin when day was gon. But lawsy, how u did fool ’em, Huck! Dat wuz de smartes’ doj! I tel u, chile, I’spec it saev’ oelay Jim—oelay Jim ain’t going to forgit u for dat, huny.”

Then we taukt about th muny. It was a prity guud raez—twenty dolars apees. Jim sed we cuud taek dek pasej on a steemboet now, and th muny wuud last us as far as we wonted to go in th free Staets. He sed twenty miel mor worn’t far for th raft to go, but he wisht we was allredy thair.

Tords daybraek we tied up, and Jim was miety particuelar about hieding th raft guud. Then he werkt all day fixing things in bundls, and geting all redy to qit rafting.

That niet about ten we hoev in siet of th liets of a toun away doun in a left-hand bend.

I went off in th canoo to ask about it. Prity soon I found a man out in th river with a skif, seting a trot-lien. I raenjd up and sez:

“Mister, is that toun Cairo?”

“Cairo? no. U must be a blaem’ fool.”

“Whut toun is it, mister?”

“If u wont to noe, go and fiend out. If u stay heer botherin’ around me for about a haf a minit longger u’ll get sumthing u wun’t wont.”

I paddled to th raft. Jim was awful disapointed, but I sed never miend, Cairo wuud be th next plaes, I rekond.

We past anuther toun befor dayliet, and I was going out agen; but it was hi ground, so I didn’t go. No hi ground about Cairo, Jim sed. I had forgot it. We laed up for th day on a toehed tolerabl cloez to th left-hand bank. I begun to suspishun sumthing. So did Jim. I sez:

“Maybe we went bi Cairo in th fog that niet.”

He sez:

“Doan’ le’s tauk about it, Huck. Po’ niggers can’t hav no luk. I awluz ’spected dat ratlsnaek-skin worn’t dun wid its werk.”

“I wish I’d never seen that snaek-skin, Jim—I do wish I’d never laed ies on it.”

“It ain’t yo’ fallt, Huck; u didn’ noe. Don’t u blaem yo’self ’bout it.”

When it was dayliet, heer was th cleer Ohio wauter inshor, shur enuf, and outsied was th oeld reguelar Mudy! So it was all up with Cairo.

We taukt it all oever. It wouldn’t do to taek to th shor; we couldn’t taek th raft up th streem, of cors. Thair worn’t no way but to waet for dark, and start bak in th canoo and taek th chanses. So we slept all day amungst th cotonwuud thiket, so as to be fresh for th werk, and when we went bak to th raft about dark th canoo was gon!

We didn’t say a werd for a guud whiel. Thair worn’t enything to say. We boeth knowed wel enuf it was sum mor werk of th ratlsnaek-skin; so whut was th ues to tauk about it? It wuud oenly luuk liek we was fiending fallt, and that wuud be bound to fech mor bad luk—and keep on feching it, too, til we knowed enuf to keep stil.

Bi and bi we taukt about whut we beter do, and found thair worn’t no way but just to go along doun with th raft til we got a chans to bi a canoo to go bak in. We worn’t going to borro it when thair worn’t enybody around, th way pap wuud do, for that miet set peepl after us.

So we shuvd out after dark on th raft.

Enybody that don’t beleev yet that it’s foolishnes to handl a snaek-skin, after all that that snaek-skin dun for us, wil beleev it now if thay red on and see whut mor it dun for us.

Th plaes to bi canoos is off of rafts laying up at shor. But we didn’t see no rafts laying up; so we went along during three ours and mor. Wel, th niet got gray and ruther thik, which is th next meenest thing to fog. U can’t tel th shaep of th river, and u can’t see no distans. It got to be verry laet and stil, and then along cums a steemboet up th river. We lit th lantern, and jujd she wuud see it. Up-streem boets didn’t generly cum cloez to us; thay go out and folo th bars and hunt for eezy wauter under th reefs; but niets liek this thay buul riet up th chanel agenst th hoel river.

We cuud heer her pounding along, but we didn’t see her guud til she was cloez. She aemd riet for us. Offen thay do that and tri to see how cloez thay can cum without tuching; sumtiems th wheel biets off a sweep, and then th pielot stiks his hed out and lafs, and thinks he’s miety smart. Wel, heer she cums, and we sed she was going to tri and shaev us; but she didn’t seem to be sheering off a bit. She was a big wun, and she was cuming in a hery, too, luuking liek a blak cloud with roes of glo-werms around it; but all of a suden she buljd out, big and scairy, with a long ro of wied-oepen fernis dors shiening liek red-hot teeth, and her monstrus boes and gards hanging riet oever us. Thair was a yel at us, and a jinggling of bels to stop th enjins, a powwow of cussing, and whisling of steem—and as Jim went oeverbord on wun sied and I on th uther, she cum smashing straet thru th raft.

I dievd—and I aemd to fiend th botom, too, for a therty-fuut wheel had got to go oever me, and I wonted it to hav plenty of room. I cuud allways stay under wauter a minit; this tiem I rekon I stayd under a minit and a haf. Then I bounst for th top in a hery, for I was neerly busting. I popt out to mi armpits and blowed th wauter out of mi noez, and puft a bit. Of cors thair was a booming curent; and of cors that boet started her enjins agen ten seconds after she stopt them, for thay never caird much for raftsmen; so now she was cherning along up th river, out of siet in th thik wether, tho I cuud heer her.

I sung out for Jim about a duzen tiems, but I didn’t get eny anser; so I grabd a plank that tucht me whiel I was “treding wauter,” and struk out for shor, shuving it ahed of me. But I maed out to see that th drift of th curent was tords th left-hand shor, which ment that I was in a crossing; so I chaenjd off and went that way.

It was wun of thees long, slanting, too-miel crossings; so I was a guud long tiem in geting oever. I maed a saef landing, and clumb up th bank. I couldn’t see but a litl ways, but I went poeking along oever ruf ground for a qorter of a miel or mor, and then I run across a big oeld-fashund dubl log-hous befor I noetist it. I was going to rush bi and get away, but a lot of daugs jumpt out and went to houling and barking at me, and I knowed beter than to moov anuther peg.

















CHAPTER XVII.

In about a minit sumbody spoek out of a windo without puuting his hed out, and sez:

“Be dun, boys! Hoo’s thair?”

I sez:

“It’s me.”

“Hoo’s me?”

“George Jackson, ser.”

“Whut do u wont?”

“I don’t wont nuthing, ser. I oenly wont to go along bi, but th daugs wun’t let me.”

“Whut ar u prouling around heer this tiem of niet for—hay?”

“I worn’t prouling around, ser, I fel oeverbord off of th steemboet.”

“O, u did, did u? Striek a liet thair, sumbody. Whut did u say yur naem was?”

“George Jackson, ser. I’m oenly a boy.”

“Luuk heer, if u’re teling th trooth u needn’t be afraed—noebody’ll hert u. But don’t tri to buj; stand riet whair u ar. Rouz out Bob and Tom, sum of u, and fech th guns. George Jackson, is thair enybody with u?”

“No, ser, noebody.”

I herd th peepl stering around in th hous now, and see a liet. Th man sung out:

“Snach that liet away, Betsy, u oeld fool—ain’t u got eny sens? Puut it on th flor behiend th frunt dor. Bob, if u and Tom ar redy, taek yur plaeses.”

“All redy.”

“Now, George Jackson, do u noe th Shepherdsons?”

“No, ser; I never herd of them.”

“Wel, that may be so, and it mayn’t. Now, all redy. Step forward, George Jackson. And miend, don’t u hery—cum miety slo. If thair’s enybody with u, let him keep bak—if he shoes himself he’ll be shot. Cum along now. Cum slo; puush th dor oepen yurself—just enuf to sqeez in, d’ u heer?”

I didn’t hery; I couldn’t if I’d a wonted to. I tuuk wun slo step at a tiem and thair worn’t a sound, oenly I thaut I cuud heer mi hart. Th daugs wer as stil as th huemans, but thay foloed a litl behiend me. When I got to th three log doorsteps I herd them unloking and unbarring and unbolting. I puut mi hand on th dor and puusht it a litl and a litl mor til sumbody sed, “Thair, that’s enuf—puut yur hed in.” I dun it, but I jujd thay wuud taek it off.

Th candl was on th flor, and thair thay all was, luuking at me, and me at them, for about a qorter of a minit: Three big men with guns pointed at me, which maed me wince, I tel u; th oeldest, gray and about sixty, th uther too therty or mor—all of them fien and hansum—and th sweetest oeld gray-heded laedy, and bak of her too yung wimen which I couldn’t see riet wel. Th oeld jentlman sez:

“Thair; I rekon it’s all riet. Cum in.”

As soon as I was in th oeld jentlman he lokt th dor and bard it and boelted it, and toeld th yung men to cum in with thair guns, and thay all went in a big parlor that had a nue rag carpet on th flor, and got together in a corner that was out of th raenj of th frunt windoes—thair worn’t nun on th sied. Thay held th candl, and tuuk a guud luuk at me, and all sed, “Whi, he ain’t a Shepherdson—no, thair ain’t eny Shepherdson about him.” Then th oeld man sed he hoept I wouldn’t miend being sercht for arms, becauz he didn’t meen no harm bi it—it was oenly to maek shur. So he didn’t pri into mi pokets, but oenly felt outsied with his hands, and sed it was all riet. He toeld me to maek mieself eezy and at hoem, and tel all about mieself; but th oeld laedy sez:

“Whi, bles u, Saul, th pur thing’s as wet as he can be; and don’t u rekon it may be he’s hunggry?”

“Troo for u, Rachel—I forgot.”

So th oeld laedy sez:

“Betsy” (this was a nigger wuuman), “u fli around and get him sumthing to eet as qik as u can, pur thing; and wun of u gerls go and waek up Buk and tel him—o, heer he is himself. Buk, taek this litl straenjer and get th wet cloeths off frum him and dres him up in sum of yurs that’s dri.”

Buk luukt about as oeld as me—therteen or forteen or along thair, tho he was a litl biger than me. He hadn’t on enything but a shert, and he was verry frouzy-heded. He caem in gaeping and diging wun fist into his ies, and he was draging a gun along with th uther wun. He sez:









“Ain’t thay no Shepherdsons around?”

Thay sed, no, ’twas a falls alarm.

“Wel,” he sez, “if thay’d a ben sum, I rekon I’d a got wun.”

Thay all laft, and Bob sez:

“Whi, Buk, thay miet hav scalped us all, u’ve bin so slo in cuming.”

“Wel, noebody cum after me, and it ain’t riet I’m allways kept doun; I don’t get no sho.”

“Never miend, Buk, mi boy,” sez th oeld man, “u’ll hav sho enuf, all in guud tiem, don’t u fret about that. Go ’long with u now, and do as yur muther toeld u.”

When we got up-stairs to his room he got me a cors shert and a roundabout and pants of his, and I puut them on. Whiel I was at it he askt me whut mi naem was, but befor I cuud tel him he started to tel me about a bluejay and a yung rabit he had catched in th wuuds day befor yesterday, and he askt me whair Moses was when th candl went out. I sed I didn’t noe; I hadn’t herd about it befor, no way.

“Wel, ges,” he sez.

“How’m I going to ges,” sez I, “when I never herd tel of it befor?”

“But u can ges, can’t u? It’s just as eezy.”

“Which candl?” I sez.

“Whi, eny candl,” he sez.

“I don’t noe whair he was,” sez I; “whair was he?”

“Whi, he was in th dark! That’s whair he was!”

“Wel, if u knowed whair he was, whut did u ask me for?”

“Whi, blaem it, it’s a ridl, don’t u see? Say, how long ar u going to stay heer? U got to stay allways. We can just hav booming tiems—thay don’t hav no scool now. Do u oen a daug? I’ve got a daug—and he’ll go in th river and bring out chips that u thro in. Do u liek to coem up Sundays, and all that kiend of foolishnes? U bet I don’t, but maa she maeks me. Confound thees oelay briches! I rekon I’d beter puut ’em on, but I’d ruther not, it’s so worm. Ar u all redy? All riet. Cum along, oeld hoss.”

Coeld corn-pone, coeld corn-beef, buter and butermilk—that is whut thay had for me doun thair, and thair ain’t nuthing beter that ever I’ve cum across yet. Buk and his maa and all of them smoekt cob pieps, exsept th nigger wuuman, which was gon, and th too yung wimen. Thay all smoekt and taukt, and I eet and taukt. Th yung wimen had qilts around them, and thair hair doun thair baks. Thay all askt me qeschuns, and I toeld them how pap and me and all th family was living on a litl farm doun at th botom of Arkansaw, and mi sister Mary Ann run off and got marryd and never was herd of no mor, and Bil went to hunt them and he worn’t herd of no mor, and Tom and Mort died, and then thair worn’t noebody but just me and pap left, and he was just trimd doun to nuthing, on acount of his trubls; so when he died I tuuk whut thair was left, becauz th farm didn’t belong to us, and started up th river, dek pasej, and fel oeverbord; and that was how I cum to be heer. So thay sed I cuud hav a hoem thair as long as I wonted it. Then it was moest dayliet and evrybody went to bed, and I went to bed with Buk, and when I waekt up in th morning, drat it all, I had forgot whut mi naem was. So I laed thair about an our trieing to think, and when Buk waekt up I sez:

“Can u spel, Buk?”

“Yes,” he sez.

“I bet u can’t spel mi naem,” sez I.

“I bet u whut u dair I can,” sez he.

“All riet,” sez I, “go ahed.”

“G-e-o-r-g-e J-a-x-o-n—thair now,” he sez.

“Wel,” sez I, “u dun it, but I didn’t think u cuud. It ain’t no slouch of a naem to spel—riet off without studying.”

I set it doun, prievet, becauz sumbody miet wont me to spel it next, and so I wonted to be handy with it and ratl it off liek I was uezd to it.

It was a miety nies family, and a miety nies hous, too. I hadn’t seen no hous out in th cuntry befor that was so nies and had so much stiel. It didn’t hav an ieern lach on th frunt dor, nor a wuuden wun with a bukskin string, but a bras nob to tern, th saem as houses in toun. Thair worn’t no bed in th parlor, nor a sien of a bed; but heeps of parlors in touns has beds in them. Thair was a big fierplaes that was bricked on th botom, and th briks was kept cleen and red bi poring wauter on them and scrubing them with anuther brik; sumtiems thay wosh them oever with red wauter-paent that thay call Spanish-broun, saem as thay do in toun. Thay had big bras daug-ieerns that cuud hoeld up a saw-log. Thair was a clok on th midl of th mantelpees, with a pikcher of a toun paented on th botom haf of th glas frunt, and a round plaes in th midl of it for th sun, and u cuud see th penjulum swinging behiend it. It was buetiful to heer that clok tik; and sumtiems when wun of thees pedlers had bin along and scourd her up and got her in guud shaep, she wuud start in and striek a hundred and fifty befor she got tuckered out. Thay wouldn’t tuuk eny muny for her.

Wel, thair was a big outlandish parrot on eech sied of th clok, maed out of sumthing liek chauk, and paented up gaudy. Bi wun of th parrots was a cat maed of crokery, and a crokery daug bi th uther; and when u prest doun on them thay sqeekt, but didn’t oepen thair mouths nor luuk diferent nor interested. Thay sqeekt thru underneeth. Thair was a cupl of big wield-terky-wing fans spred out behiend thoes things. On th taebl in th midl of th room was a kiend of a luvly crokery basket that had apls and orenjes and peeches and graeps pield up in it, which was much reder and yellower and prityer than reeal wuns is, but thay worn’t reeal becauz u cuud see whair peeses had got chipt off and shoed th whiet chauk, or whutever it was, underneeth.

This taebl had a cuver maed out of buetiful oilclauth, with a red and bloo spred-eegl paented on it, and a paented border all around. It cum all th way frum Philadelphia, thay sed. Thair was sum buuks, too, pield up perfectly exact, on eech corner of th taebl. Wun was a big family Biebl fuul of pikchers. Wun was Pilgrim’s Progres, about a man that left his family, it didn’t say whi. I red considerabl in it now and then. Th staetments was interesting, but tuf. Anuther was Frendship’s Offering, fuul of buetiful stuf and poeetry; but I didn’t red th poeetry. Anuther was Henry Clay’s Speeches, and anuther was Dr. Gunn’s Family Medisin, which toeld u all about whut to do if a body was sik or ded. Thair was a him buuk, and a lot of uther buuks. And thair was nies split-botom chairs, and perfectly sound, too—not bagd doun in th midl and busted, liek an oeld basket.

Thay had pikchers hung on th walls—maenly Washingtons and Lafayettes, and batls, and Hieland Marys, and wun calld “Siening th Declaraeshun.” Thair was sum that thay calld crayons, which wun of th dauters which was ded maed her oen self when she was oenly fifteen yeers oeld. Thay was diferent frum eny pikchers I ever see befor—blacker, moestly, than is comon. Wun was a wuuman in a slim blak dres, belted small under th armpits, with bulges liek a cabej in th midl of th sleevs, and a larj blak scoop-shuvel bonet with a blak vael, and whiet slim ankls crosst about with blak taep, and verry wee blak slipers, liek a chizel, and she was leening pensiv on a toomstoen on her riet elbo, under a weeping wilo, and her uther hand hanging doun her sied hoelding a whiet hankerchif and a reticuel, and underneeth th pikcher it sed “Shal I Never See Thee Mor Alas.” Anuther wun was a yung laedy with her hair all coemd up straet to th top of her hed, and noted thair in frunt of a coem liek a chair-bak, and she was crieing into a hankerchif and had a ded berd laying on its bak in her uther hand with its heels up, and underneeth th pikcher it sed “I Shal Never Heer Thi Sweet Chirrup Mor Alas.” Thair was wun whair a yung laedy was at a windo luuking up at th moon, and teers runing doun her cheeks; and she had an oepen leter in wun hand with blak seeling wax shoeing on wun ej of it, and she was mashing a locket with a chaen to it agenst her mouth, and underneeth th pikcher it sed “And Art Thow Gon Yes Thow Art Gon Alas.” Thees was all nies pikchers, I rekon, but I didn’t sumhow seem to taek to them, becauz if ever I was doun a litl thay allways giv me th fan-tods. Evrybody was sorry she died, becauz she had laed out a lot mor of thees pikchers to do, and a body cuud see bi whut she had dun whut thay had lost. But I rekond that with her dispozishun she was having a beter tiem in th graev-yard. She was at werk on whut thay sed was her graetest pikcher when she tuuk sik, and evry day and evry niet it was her prair to be alowd to liv til she got it dun, but she never got th chans. It was a pikcher of a yung wuuman in a long whiet goun, standing on th rael of a brij all redy to jump off, with her hair all doun her bak, and luuking up to th moon, with th teers runing doun her faes, and she had too arms foelded across her brest, and too arms strecht out in frunt, and too mor reeching up tords th moon—and th iedeea was to see which pair wuud luuk best, and then scrach out all th uther arms; but, as I was saying, she died befor she got her miend maed up, and now thay kept this pikcher oever th hed of th bed in her room, and evry tiem her berthday cum thay hung flowers on it. Uther tiems it was hid with a litl curten. Th yung wuuman in th pikcher had a kiend of a nies sweet faes, but thair was so meny arms it maed her luuk too spiedery, seemd to me.





















This yung gerl kept a scrap-buuk when she was aliev, and uezd to paest obichuairys and acsidents and caeses of paeshent sufering in it out of th Presbyterian Obzerver, and riet poeetry after them out of her oen hed. It was verry guud poeetry. This is whut she roet about a boy bi th naem of Stephen Dowling Bots that fel doun a wel and was drownded:

Oed TO STEPHEN DOWLING BOTS, DEC’D

And did yung Stephen sicken,
And did yung Stephen die?
And did th sad harts thiken,
And did th morners cri?

No; such was not th faet of
Yung Stephen Dowling Bots;
Tho sad harts round him thikend,
’Twas not frum siknes’ shots.

No hooping-cauf did rak his fraem,
Nor meezls drear with spots;
Not thees impaird th saecred naem
Of Stephen Dowling Bots.

Despiezd luv struk not with woe
That hed of curly nots,
Nor stumac trubls laed him lo,
Yung Stephen Dowling Bots.

O no. Then list with teerful ie,
Whilst I his faet do tel.
His soel did frum this coeld werld fli
Bi falling doun a wel.

Thay got him out and emptyd him;
Alas it was too laet;
His spirit was gon for to sport alofft
In th relms of th guud and graet.

If Emmeline Grangerford cuud maek poeetry liek that befor she was forteen, thair ain’t no teling whut she cuud a dun bi and bi. Buk sed she cuud ratl off poeetry liek nuthing. She didn’t ever hav to stop to think. He sed she wuud slap doun a lien, and if she couldn’t fiend enything to riem with it wuud just scrach it out and slap doun anuther wun, and go ahed. She worn’t particuelar; she cuud riet about enything u chooz to giv her to riet about just so it was sadful. Evry tiem a man died, or a wuuman died, or a chield died, she wuud be on hand with her “tribuet” befor he was coeld. She calld them tribuets. Th naebors sed it was th doctor ferst, then Emmeline, then th undertaeker—th undertaeker never got in ahed of Emmeline but wuns, and then she hung fier on a riem for th ded person’s naem, which was Whistler. She worn’t ever th saem after that; she never complaend, but she kiender pined away and did not liv long. Pur thing, meny’s th tiem I maed mieself go up to th litl room that uezd to be hers and get out her pur oeld scrap-buuk and red in it when her pikchers had bin agravaeting me and I had soured on her a litl. I liekt all that family, ded wuns and all, and worn’t going to let enything cum between us. Pur Emmeline maed poeetry about all th ded peepl when she was aliev, and it didn’t seem riet that thair worn’t noebody to maek sum about her now she was gon; so I tried to swet out a vers or too mieself, but I couldn’t seem to maek it go sumhow. Thay kept Emmeline’s room trim and nies, and all th things fixt in it just th way she liekt to hav them when she was aliev, and noebody ever slept thair. Th oeld laedy tuuk cair of th room herself, tho thair was plenty of niggers, and she soed thair a guud deel and red her Biebl thair moestly.

Wel, as I was saying about th parlor, thair was buetiful curtens on th windoes: whiet, with pikchers paented on them of casls with viens all doun th walls, and catl cuming doun to drink. Thair was a litl oeld peano, too, that had tin pans in it, I rekon, and nuthing was ever so luvly as to heer th yung laedys sing “Th Last Link is Broeken” and play “Th Batl of Prague” on it. Th walls of all th rooms was plasterd, and moest had carpets on th flors, and th hoel hous was whietwosht on th outsied.

It was a dubl hous, and th big oepen plaes betwixt them was rooft and floored, and sumtiems th taebl was set thair in th midl of th day, and it was a cool, cumfortabl plaes. Nuthing couldn’t be beter. And worn’t th cuuking guud, and just buushels of it too!




















CHAPTER XVIII.

Col. Grangerford was a jentlman, u see. He was a jentlman all oever; and so was his family. He was wel born, as th saying is, and that’s werth as much in a man as it is in a hors, so th Wido Douglas sed, and noebody ever denied that she was of th ferst arristocrasy in our toun; and pap he allways sed it, too, tho he worn’t no mor qolity than a mudcat himself. Col. Grangerford was verry tall and verry slim, and had a darkish-paly complexshun, not a sien of red in it anywheres; he was cleen shaevd evry morning all oever his thin faes, and he had th thinnest kiend of lips, and th thinnest kiend of nostrils, and a hi noez, and hevy iebrows, and th blakest kiend of ies, sunk so deep bak that thay seemd liek thay was luuking out of caverns at u, as u may say. His forhed was hi, and his hair was blak and straet and hung to his shoelders. His hands was long and thin, and evry day of his lief he puut on a cleen shert and a fuul soot frum hed to fuut maed out of linen so whiet it hert yur ies to luuk at it; and on Sundays he wor a bloo tael-coet with bras butons on it. He carryd a mahogany caen with a silver hed to it. Thair worn’t no frivolishness about him, not a bit, and he worn’t ever loud. He was as kiend as he cuud be—u cuud feel that, u noe, and so u had confidens. Sumtiems he smield, and it was guud to see; but when he straetend himself up liek a liberty-poel, and th lietning begun to fliker out frum under his iebrows, u wonted to cliem a tree ferst, and fiend out whut th mater was afterwards. He didn’t ever hav to tel enybody to miend thair maners—evrybody was allways guud-manerd whair he was. Evrybody luvd to hav him around, too; he was sunshien moest allways—I meen he maed it seem liek guud wether. When he ternd into a cloudbank it was awful dark for haf a minit, and that was enuf; thair wouldn’t nuthing go rong agen for a week.

When him and th oeld laedy cum doun in th morning all th family got up out of thair chairs and giv them guud-day, and didn’t set doun agen til thay had set doun. Then Tom and Bob went to th siedbord whair th decanter was, and mixt a glas of biters and handed it to him, and he held it in his hand and waeted til Tom’s and Bob’s was mixt, and then thay boed and sed, “Our duety to u, ser, and madam;” and thay boed th leest bit in th werld and sed thank u, and so thay drank, all three, and Bob and Tom pord a spoonful of wauter on th shuugar and th miet of whisky or apl brandy in th botom of thair tumblers, and giv it to me and Buk, and we drank to th oeld peepl too.

Bob was th oeldest and Tom next—tall, buetiful men with verry braud shoelders and broun faeses, and long blak hair and blak ies. Thay drest in whiet linen frum hed to fuut, liek th oeld jentlman, and wor braud Panama hats.

Then thair was Mis Charlotte; she was twenty-fiev, and tall and proud and grand, but as guud as she cuud be when she worn’t sterd up; but when she was she had a luuk that wuud maek u wilt in yur traks, liek her faather. She was buetiful.

So was her sister, Mis Sophia, but it was a diferent kiend. She was jentl and sweet liek a doev, and she was oenly twenty.

Eech person had thair oen nigger to waet on them—Buk too. Mi nigger had a monstrus eezy tiem, becauz I worn’t uezd to having enybody do enything for me, but Buk’s was on th jump moest of th tiem.

This was all thair was of th family now, but thair uezd to be mor—three suns; thay got kild; and Emmeline that died.

Th oeld jentlman oend a lot of farms and oever a hundred niggers. Sumtiems a stak of peepl wuud cum thair, horsbak, frum ten or fifteen miel around, and stay fiev or six days, and hav such junketings round about and on th river, and danses and picniks in th wuuds daytimes, and balls at th hous niets. Thees peepl was moestly kinfolks of th family. Th men braut thair guns with them. It was a hansum lot of qolity, I tel u.

Thair was anuther clan of arristocrasy around thair—fiev or six familys—moestly of th naem of Shepherdson. Thay was as hi-toned and wel born and rich and grand as th trieb of Grangerfords. Th Shepherdsons and Grangerfords uezd th saem steemboet landing, which was about too miel abuv our hous; so sumtiems when I went up thair with a lot of our foeks I uezd to see a lot of th Shepherdsons thair on thair fien horses.

Wun day Buk and me was away out in th wuuds hunting, and herd a hors cuming. We was crossing th roed. Buk sez:

“Qik! Jump for th wuuds!”











We dun it, and then peeped doun th wuuds thru th leevs. Prity soon a splendid yung man cum galoping doun th roed, seting his hors eezy and luuking liek a soeljer. He had his gun across his pumel. I had seen him befor. It was yung Harney Shepherdson. I herd Buk’s gun go off at mi eer, and Harney’s hat tumbld off frum his hed. He grabd his gun and roed straet to th plaes whair we was hid. But we didn’t waet. We started thru th wuuds on a run. Th wuuds worn’t thik, so I luukt oever mi shoelder to doj th buulet, and twies I seen Harney cuver Buk with his gun; and then he roed away th way he cum—to get his hat, I rekon, but I couldn’t see. We never stopt runing til we got hoem. Th oeld jentlman’s ies blaezd a minit—’twas plezher, maenly, I jujd—then his faes sort of smoothd doun, and he sez, kiend of jentl:

“I don’t liek that shooting frum behiend a buush. Whi didn’t u step into th roed, mi boy?”

“Th Shepherdsons don’t, faather. Thay allways taek advantej.”

Mis Charlotte she held her hed up liek a qeen whiel Buk was teling his tael, and her nostrils spred and her ies snapt. Th too yung men luukt dark, but never sed nuthing. Mis Sophia she ternd pael, but th culor cum bak when she found th man worn’t hert.











Soon as I cuud get Buk doun bi th corn-cribs under th trees bi ourselvs, I sez:

“Did u wont to kil him, Buk?”

“Wel, I bet I did.”

“Whut did he do to u?”

“Him? He never dun nuthing to me.”

“Wel, then, whut did u wont to kil him for?”

“Whi, nuthing—oenly it’s on acount of th fued.”

“Whut’s a fued?”

“Whi, whair was u raezd? Don’t u noe whut a fued is?”

“Never herd of it befor—tel me about it.”

“Wel,” sez Buk, “a fued is this way: A man has a qorrel with anuther man, and kils him; then that uther man’s bruther kils him; then th uther bruthers, on boeth sieds, goes for wun anuther; then th cuzins chip in—and bi and bi evrybody’s kild off, and thair ain’t no mor fued. But it’s kiend of slo, and taeks a long tiem.”

“Has this wun bin going on long, Buk?”

“Wel, I shuud rekon! It started therty yeer ago, or som’ers along thair. Thair was trubl ’bout sumthing, and then a lawsoot to setl it; and th soot went agin wun of th men, and so he up and shot th man that wun th soot—which he wuud nacheraly do, of cors. Enybody wuud.”

“Whut was th trubl about, Buk?—land?”

“I rekon maybe—I don’t noe.”

“Wel, hoo dun th shooting? Was it a Grangerford or a Shepherdson?”

“Laws, how do I noe? It was so long ago.”

“Don’t enybody noe?”

“O, yes, paa noes, I rekon, and sum of th uther oeld peepl; but thay don’t noe now whut th ro was about in th ferst plaes.”

“Has thair bin meny kild, Buk?”

“Yes; riet smart chans of fuenerals. But thay don’t allways kil. Paa’s got a fue bukshot in him; but he don’t miend it ’cuz he don’t way much, enyway. Bob’s bin carvd up sum with a bowie, and Tom’s bin hert wuns or twies.”

“Has enybody bin kild this yeer, Buk?”

“Yes; we got wun and thay got wun. ’Bout three munths ago mi cuzin Bud, forteen yeer oeld, was rieding thru th wuuds on t’uther sied of th river, and didn’t hav no wepon with him, which was blaem’ foolishnes, and in a loensum plaes he heers a hors a-cuming behiend him, and sees oeld Balldy Shepherdson a-linkin’ after him with his gun in his hand and his whiet hair a-flieing in th wiend; and ’sted of jumping off and taeking to th brush, Bud ’lowed he cuud out-run him; so thay had it, nip and tuk, for fiev miel or mor, th oeld man a-gaening all th tiem; so at last Bud seen it worn’t eny ues, so he stopt and faest around so as to hav th buulet hoels in frunt, u noe, and th oeld man he roed up and shot him doun. But he didn’t git much chans to enjoy his luk, for insied of a week our foeks laed him out.”

“I rekon that oeld man was a coward, Buk.”

“I rekon he worn’t a coward. Not bi a blaem’ siet. Thair ain’t a coward amungst them Shepherdsons—not a wun. And thair ain’t no cowards amungst th Grangerfords eether. Whi, that oeld man kep’ up his end in a fiet wun day for haf an our agenst three Grangerfords, and cum out winer. Thay was all a-horsbak; he lit off of his hors and got behiend a litl wuudpiel, and kep’ his hors befor him to stop th buulets; but th Grangerfords stayd on thair horses and capered around th oeld man, and peperd away at him, and he peperd away at them. Him and his hors boeth went hoem prity leeky and cripld, but th Grangerfords had to be fetched hoem—and wun of ’em was ded, and anuther died th next day. No, ser; if a body’s out hunting for cowards he don’t wont to fool away eny tiem amungst them Shepherdsons, becuz thay don’t breed eny of that kiend.”

Next Sunday we all went to cherch, about three miel, evrybody a-horsbak. Th men tuuk thair guns along, so did Buk, and kept them between thair nees or stuud them handy agenst th wall. Th Shepherdsons dun th saem. It was prity ornery preeching—all about brutherly luv, and such-liek tiresomeness; but evrybody sed it was a guud sermon, and thay all taukt it oever going hoem, and had such a powerful lot to say about faeth and guud werks and free graes and preforeordestination, and I don’t noe whut all, that it did seem to me to be wun of th rufest Sundays I had run across yet.

About an our after diner evrybody was doezing around, sum in thair chairs and sum in thair rooms, and it got to be prity dul. Buk and a daug was strecht out on th gras in th sun sound asleep. I went up to our room, and jujd I wuud taek a nap mieself. I found that sweet Mis Sophia standing in her dor, which was next to ours, and she tuuk me in her room and shut th dor verry sofft, and askt me if I liekt her, and I sed I did; and she askt me if I wuud do sumthing for her and not tel enybody, and I sed I wuud. Then she sed she’d forgot her Testament, and left it in th seet at cherch between too uther buuks, and wuud I slip out qieet and go thair and fech it to her, and not say nuthing to noebody. I sed I wuud. So I slid out and slipt off up th roed, and thair worn’t enybody at th cherch, exsept maybe a hog or too, for thair worn’t eny lok on th dor, and hogs lieks a punchon flor in sumer-tiem becauz it’s cool. If u noetis, moest foeks don’t go to cherch oenly when thay’ve got to; but a hog is diferent.











Sez I to mieself, sumthing’s up; it ain’t nacheral for a gerl to be in such a swet about a Testament. So I giv it a shaek, and out drops a litl pees of paeper with “Haf-PAST Too” roet on it with a pensil. I ransakt it, but couldn’t fiend enything els. I couldn’t maek enything out of that, so I puut th paeper in th buuk agen, and when I got hoem and upstairs thair was Mis Sophia in her dor waeting for me. She puuld me in and shut th dor; then she luukt in th Testament til she found th paeper, and as soon as she red it she luukt glad; and befor a body cuud think she grabd me and giv me a sqeez, and sed I was th best boy in th werld, and not to tel enybody. She was miety red in th faes for a minit, and her ies lieted up, and it maed her powerful prity. I was a guud deel astonisht, but when I got mi breth I askt her whut th paeper was about, and she askt me if I had red it, and I sed no, and she askt me if I cuud red rieting, and I toeld her “no, oenly cors-hand,” and then she sed th paeper worn’t enything but a buuk-mark to keep her plaes, and I miet go and play now.

I went off doun to th river, studying oever this thing, and prity soon I noetist that mi nigger was foloeing along behiend. When we was out of siet of th hous he luukt bak and around a second, and then cums a-runing, and sez:

“Mars Jawge, if u’ll cum doun into de swomp I’ll sho u a hoel stak o’ wauter-mocasins.”

Thinks I, that’s miety cuerius; he sed that yesterday. He oughter noe a body don’t luv wauter-mocasins enuf to go around hunting for them. Whut is he up to, enyway? So I sez:

“All riet; trot ahed.”

I foloed a haf a miel; then he struk out oever th swomp, and waeded ankl deep as much as anuther haf-miel. We cum to a litl flat pees of land which was dri and verry thik with trees and buushes and viens, and he sez:

“U shuv riet in dah jist a fue steps, Mars Jawge; dah’s whah day is. I’s seed ’m befo’; I don’t k’yer to see ’em no mo’.”

Then he slopt riet along and went away, and prity soon th trees hid him. I poekt into th plaes a-ways and cum to a litl oepen pach as big as a bedroom all hung around with viens, and found a man laying thair asleep—and, bi jings, it was mi oeld Jim!

I waekt him up, and I rekond it was going to be a grand serpriez to him to see me agen, but it worn’t. He neerly cried he was so glad, but he worn’t serpriezd. Sed he swum along behiend me that niet, and herd me yel evry tiem, but dasn’t anser, becauz he didn’t wont noebody to pik him up and taek him into slaevery agen. Sez he:

“I got hert a litl, en couldn’t swim fas’, so I wuz a considable ways behine u tords de las’; when u landed I reck’ned I cuud ketch up wid u on de lan’ ’dout havin’ to shout at u, but when I see dat hous I begin to go slo. I ’uz off too fer to heer whut day say to u—I wuz ’fraid o’ de daugs; but when it ’uz all qieet agin I knowed u’s in de hous, so I struk out for de wuuds to waet for day. Erly in de mawnin’ sum er de niggers cum along, gwyne to de feelds, en day tuk me en shoed me dis plaes, whah de daugs can’t trak me on acounts o’ de wauter, en day brings me truk to eet evry niet, en tels me how u’s a-gitt’n along.”

“Whi didn’t u tel mi Jak to fech me heer sooner, Jim?”

“Wel, ’twarn’t no ues to ’sturb u, Huck, tel we cuud do sumfn—but we’s all riet now. I ben a-buyin’ pots en pans en vittles, as I got a chanst, en a-patchin’ up de raf’ niets when—”

“Whut raft, Jim?”

“Our oelay raf’.”

“U meen to say our oeld raft worn’t smasht all to flinders?”

“No, she worn’t. She was tore up a guud deel—wun en’ of her was; but day worn’t no graet harm dun, on’y our traps was mos’ all los’. Ef we hadn’ diev’ so deep en swum so fer under wauter, en de niet hadn’ ben so dark, en we worn’t so sk’yerd, en ben sich punkin-heds, as de sayin’ is, we’d a seed de raf’. But it’s jis’ as wel we didn’t, ’kase now she’s all fixt up agin mos’ as guud as nue, en we’s got a nue lot o’ stuf, in de plaes o’ whut ’uz los’.”

“Whi, how did u get hoeld of th raft agen, Jim—did u cach her?”

“How I gwyne to ketch her en I out in de wuuds? No; sum er de niggers foun’ her ketched on a snag along heah in de ben’, en day hid her in a crik ’mongst de wiloes, en day wuz so much jawin’ ’bout which un ’um she b’long to de mos’ dat I cum to heah ’bout it pooty soon, so I ups en setls de trubl bi tellin’ ’um she don’t b’long to nun uv um, but to u en me; en I ast ’m if day gwyne to grab a yung whiet genlman’s propaty, en git a hid’n for it? Den I jin ’m ten sents apees, en day ’uz miety wel satisfied, en wisht sum mo’ raf’s ’ud cum along en maek ’m rich agin. Day’s miety guud to me, dese niggers is, en whutever I wonts ’m to do fer me I doan’ hav to ast ’m twies, huny. Dat Jak’s a guud nigger, en pooty smart.”

“Yes, he is. He ain’t ever toeld me u was heer; toeld me to cum, and he’d sho me a lot of wauter-mocasins. If enything hapens he ain’t mixt up in it. He can say he never seen us together, and it ’ll be th trooth.”

I don’t wont to tauk much about th next day. I rekon I’ll cut it prity short. I waekt up about daun, and was a-going to tern oever and go to sleep agen when I noetist how stil it was—didn’t seem to be enybody stering. That worn’t uezhual. Next I noetist that Buk was up and gon. Wel, I gets up, a-wundering, and goes doun stairs—noebody around; evrything as stil as a mous. Just th saem outsied. Thinks I, whut duz it meen? Doun bi th wuud-piel I cums across mi Jak, and sez:

“Whut’s it all about?”

Sez he:

“Don’t u noe, Mars Jawge?”

“No,” sez I, “I don’t.”

“Wel, den, Mis Sophia’s run off! ’deed she has. She run off in de niet sum tiem—noebody don’t noe jis’ when; run off to get marryd to dat yung Harney Shepherdson, u noe—leastways, so day ’spec. De fambly foun’ it out ’bout haf an our ago—maybe a litl mo’—en’ I tel u day worn’t no tiem los’. Sich anuther hurryin’ up guns en hosses u never see! De wimen foeks has gon for to ster up de relaeshuns, en oelay Mars Saul en de boys tuk day guns en roed up de river roed for to tri to ketch dat yung man en kil him ’fo’ he kin git acrost de river wid Mis Sophia. I reck’n day’s gwyne to be miety ruf tiems.”

“Buk went off ’thout waeking me up.”

“Wel, I reck’n he did! Day worn’t gwyne to mix u up in it. Mars Buk he loeded up his gun en ’lowed he’s gwyne to fech hoem a Shepherdson or bust. Wel, day’ll be plenty un ’m dah, I reck’n, en u bet u he’ll fech wun ef he gits a chanst.”

I tuuk up th river roed as hard as I cuud puut. Bi and bi I begin to heer guns a guud ways off. When I cum in siet of th log stor and th wuudpiel whair th steamboats lands I werkt along under th trees and brush til I got to a guud plaes, and then I clumb up into th forks of a cotonwuud that was out of reech, and wocht. Thair was a wuud-rank foer fuut hi a litl ways in frunt of th tree, and ferst I was going to hied behiend that; but maybe it was lukyer I didn’t.

Thair was foer or fiev men cavorting around on thair horses in th oepen plaes befor th log stor, cussing and yeling, and trieing to get at a cupl of yung chaps that was behiend th wuud-rank alongsied of th steemboet landing; but thay couldn’t cum it. Evry tiem wun of them shoed himself on th river sied of th wuudpiel he got shot at. Th too boys was sqoting bak to bak behiend th piel, so thay cuud woch boeth ways.











Bi and bi th men stopt cavorting around and yeling. Thay started rieding tords th stor; then up gets wun of th boys, draws a stedy beed oever th wuud-rank, and drops wun of them out of his sadl. All th men jumpt off of thair horses and grabd th hert wun and started to carry him to th stor; and that minit th too boys started on th run. Thay got haf way to th tree I was in befor th men noetist. Then th men see them, and jumpt on thair horses and tuuk out after them. Thay gaend on th boys, but it didn’t do no guud, th boys had too guud a start; thay got to th wuudpiel that was in frunt of mi tree, and slipt in behiend it, and so thay had th bulj on th men agen. Wun of th boys was Buk, and th uther was a slim yung chap about nienteen yeers oeld.

Th men ript around awhiel, and then roed away. As soon as thay was out of siet I sung out to Buk and toeld him. He didn’t noe whut to maek of mi vois cuming out of th tree at ferst. He was awful serpriezd. He toeld me to woch out sharp and let him noe when th men cum in siet agen; sed thay was up to sum devilment or uther—wouldn’t be gon long. I wisht I was out of that tree, but I dasn’t cum doun. Buk begun to cri and rip, and ’lowed that him and his cuzin Joe (that was th uther yung chap) wuud maek up for this day yet. He sed his faather and his too bruthers was kild, and too or three of th enemy. Sed th Shepherdsons laed for them in ambush. Buk sed his faather and bruthers aut to waeted for thair relaeshuns—th Shepherdsons was too strong for them. I askt him whut was becum of yung Harney and Mis Sophia. He sed thay’d got across th river and was saef. I was glad of that; but th way Buk did taek on becauz he didn’t manej to kil Harney that day he shot at him—I hain’t ever herd enything liek it.

All of a suden, bang! bang! bang! goes three or foer guns—th men had slipt around thru th wuuds and cum in frum behiend without thair horses! Th boys jumpt for th river—boeth of them hert—and as thay swum doun th curent th men run along th bank shooting at them and singing out, “Kil them, kil them!” It maed me so sik I moest fel out of th tree. I ain’t a-going to tel all that hapend—it wuud maek me sik agen if I was to do that. I wisht I hadn’t ever cum ashor that niet to see such things. I ain’t ever going to get shut of them—lots of tiems I dreem about them.

I stayd in th tree til it begun to get dark, afraed to cum doun. Sumtiems I herd guns away off in th wuuds; and twies I seen litl gangs of men galop past th log stor with guns; so I rekond th trubl was stil a-going on. I was miety dounharted; so I maed up mi miend I wouldn’t ever go anear that hous agen, becauz I rekond I was to blaem, sumhow. I jujd that that pees of paeper ment that Mis Sophia was to meet Harney somewheres at haf-past too and run off; and I jujd I aut to toeld her faather about that paeper and th cuerius way she acted, and then maybe he wuud a lokt her up, and this awful mes wouldn’t ever hapend.

When I got doun out of th tree I crept along doun th river bank a pees, and found th too bodys laying in th ej of th wauter, and tugd at them til I got them ashor; then I cuverd up thair faeses, and got away as qik as I cuud. I cried a litl when I was cuvering up Buk’s faes, for he was miety guud to me.

It was just dark now. I never went neer th hous, but struk thru th wuuds and maed for th swomp. Jim worn’t on his ieland, so I trampt off in a hery for th crik, and crouded thru th wiloes, red-hot to jump abord and get out of that awful cuntry. Th raft was gon! Mi soels, but I was scaird! I couldn’t get mi breth for moest a minit. Then I raezd a yel. A vois not twenty-fiev fuut frum me sez:

“Guud lan’! is dat u, huny? Doan’ maek no noiz.”

It was Jim’s vois—nuthing ever sounded so guud befor. I run along th bank a pees and got abord, and Jim he grabd me and hugd me, he was so glad to see me. He sez:

“Laws bles u, chile, I ’uz riet doun sho’ u’s ded agin. Jak’s bin heah; he say he reck’n u’s ben shot, kase u didn’ cum hoem no mo’; so I’s jes’ dis minit a startin’ de raf’ doun tords de mouf er de crik, so’s to be all redy for to shuv out en leev soon as Jak cums agin en tels me for serten u is ded. Lawsy, I’s miety glad to git u bak agen, huny.”

I sez:

“All riet—that’s miety guud; thay wun’t fiend me, and thay’ll think I’ve bin kild, and floeted doun th river—thair’s sumthing up thair that ’ll help them think so—so don’t u looz no tiem, Jim, but just shuv off for th big wauter as fast as ever u can.”

I never felt eezy til th raft was too miel belo thair and out in th midl of th Mississippi. Then we hung up our signal lantern, and jujd that we was free and saef wuns mor. I hadn’t had a biet to eet sinss yesterday, so Jim he got out sum corn-dojers and butermilk, and pork and cabej and greens—thair ain’t nuthing in th werld so guud when it’s cuukt riet—and whilst I eet mi super we taukt and had a guud tiem. I was powerful glad to get away frum th fueds, and so was Jim to get away frum th swomp. We sed thair worn’t no hoem liek a raft, after all. Uther plaeses do seem so cramped up and smothery, but a raft don’t. U feel miety free and eezy and cumfortabl on a raft.













CHAPTER XIX.

Too or three days and niets went bi; I rekon I miet say thay swum bi, thay slid along so qieet and smooth and luvly. Heer is th way we puut in th tiem. It was a monstrus big river doun thair—sumtiems a miel and a haf wied; we run niets, and laed up and hid daytimes; soon as niet was moest gon we stopt navigaeting and tied up—neerly allways in th ded wauter under a toehed; and then cut yung cottonwoods and wiloes, and hid th raft with them. Then we set out th liens. Next we slid into th river and had a swim, so as to freshen up and cool off; then we set doun on th sandy botom whair th wauter was about nee deep, and wocht th dayliet cum. Not a sound anywheres—perfectly stil—just liek th hoel werld was asleep, oenly sumtiems th bullfrogs a-cluttering, maybe. Th ferst thing to see, luuking away oever th wauter, was a kiend of dul lien—that was th wuuds on t’uther sied; u couldn’t maek nuthing els out; then a pael plaes in th skie; then mor paelnes spreding around; then th river soffend up away off, and worn’t blak eny mor, but gray; u cuud see litl dark spots drifting along ever so far away—traeding scows, and such things; and long blak streeks—rafts; sumtiems u cuud heer a sweep screaking; or jumbld up voises, it was so stil, and sounds cum so far; and bi and bi u cuud see a streek on th wauter which u noe bi th luuk of th streek that thair’s a snag thair in a swift curent which braeks on it and maeks that streek luuk that way; and u see th mist curl up off of th wauter, and th eest reddens up, and th river, and u maek out a log-cabin in th ej of th wuuds, away on th bank on t’uther sied of th river, being a wuud-yard, liekly, and pield bi them cheats so u can thro a daug thru it anywheres; then th nies breez springs up, and cums faning u frum oever thair, so cool and fresh and sweet to smel on acount of th wuuds and th flowers; but sumtiems not that way, becauz thay’ve left ded fish laying around, gars and such, and thay do get prity rank; and next u’ve got th fuul day, and evrything smieling in th sun, and th song-berds just going it!

A litl smoek couldn’t be noetist now, so we wuud taek sum fish off of th liens and cuuk up a hot brekfast. And afterwards we wuud woch th lonesomeness of th river, and kiend of laezy along, and bi and bi laezy off to sleep. Waek up bi and bi, and luuk to see whut dun it, and maybe see a steemboet caufing along up-streem, so far off tords th uther sied u couldn’t tel nuthing about her oenly whether she was a stern-wheel or sied-wheel; then for about an our thair wouldn’t be nuthing to heer nor nuthing to see—just solid lonesomeness. Next u’d see a raft slieding bi, away off yonder, and maybe a galoot on it choping, becauz thay’re moest allways doing it on a raft; u’d see th ax flash and cum doun—u don’t heer nuthing; u see that ax go up agen, and bi th tiem it’s abuv th man’s hed then u heer th k’chunk!—it had tuuk all that tiem to cum oever th wauter. So we wuud puut in th day, lazying around, lisening to th stilnes. Wuns thair was a thik fog, and th rafts and things that went bi was beeting tin pans so th steamboats wouldn’t run oever them. A scow or a raft went bi so cloez we cuud heer them tauking and cussing and lafing—herd them plaen; but we couldn’t see no sien of them; it maed u feel crawly; it was liek spirits carrying on that way in th air. Jim sed he beleevd it was spirits; but I sez:

“No; spirits wouldn’t say, ‘Dern th dern fog.’”

Soon as it was niet out we shuvd; when we got her out to about th midl we let her aloen, and let her floet whairever th curent wonted her to; then we lit th pieps, and danggld our legs in th wauter, and taukt about all kiends of things—we was allways naeked, day and niet, whenever th moskeetoes wuud let us—th nue cloeths Buk’s foeks maed for me was too guud to be cumfortabl, and besieds I didn’t go much on cloeths, nohow.

Sumtiems we’d hav that hoel river all to ourselvs for th longgest tiem. Yonder was th banks and th ielands, across th wauter; and maybe a spark—which was a candl in a cabin windo; and sumtiems on th wauter u cuud see a spark or too—on a raft or a scow, u noe; and maybe u cuud heer a fidl or a song cuming oever frum wun of them crafts. It’s luvly to liv on a raft. We had th skie up thair, all spekld with stars, and we uezd to lay on our baks and luuk up at them, and discus about whether thay was maed or oenly just hapend. Jim he alowd thay was maed, but I alowd thay hapend; I jujd it wuud hav tuuk too long to maek so meny. Jim sed th moon cuud a laed them; wel, that luukt kiend of reezonabl, so I didn’t say nuthing agenst it, becauz I’ve seen a frog lay moest as meny, so of cors it cuud be dun. We uezd to woch th stars that fel, too, and see them streek doun. Jim alowd thay’d got spoild and was hoev out of th nest.

Wuns or twies of a niet we wuud see a steemboet sliping along in th dark, and now and then she wuud belsh a hoel werld of sparks up out of her chimbleys, and thay wuud raen doun in th river and luuk awful prity; then she wuud tern a corner and her liets wuud wink out and her powwow shut off and leev th river stil agen; and bi and bi her waevs wuud get to us, a long tiem after she was gon, and jogl th raft a bit, and after that u wouldn’t heer nuthing for u couldn’t tel how long, exsept maybe frogs or sumthing.

After midniet th peepl on shor went to bed, and then for too or three ours th shors was blak—no mor sparks in th cabin windoes. Thees sparks was our clok—th ferst wun that shoed agen ment morning was cuming, so we hunted a plaes to hied and tee up riet away.

Wun morning about daybraek I found a canoo and crosst oever a shoot to th maen shor—it was oenly too hundred yards—and paddled about a miel up a crik amungst th siepres wuuds, to see if I couldn’t get sum berrys. Just as I was pasing a plaes whair a kiend of a cowpath crosst th crik, heer cums a cupl of men tairing up th path as tiet as thay cuud fuut it. I thaut I was a goner, for whenever enybody was after enybody I jujd it was me—or maybe Jim. I was about to dig out frum thair in a hery, but thay was prity cloez to me then, and sung out and begd me to saev thair lievs—sed thay hadn’t bin doing nuthing, and was being chaest for it—sed thair was men and daugs a-cuming. Thay wonted to jump riet in, but I sez:











“Don’t u do it. I don’t heer th daugs and horses yet; u’ve got tiem to croud thru th brush and get up th crik a litl ways; then u taek to th wauter and waed doun to me and get in—that’ll thro th daugs off th sent.”

Thay dun it, and soon as thay was abord I lit out for our toehed, and in about fiev or ten minits we herd th daugs and th men away off, shouting. We herd them cum along tords th crik, but couldn’t see them; thay seemd to stop and fool around a whiel; then, as we got ferther and ferther away all th tiem, we couldn’t hardly heer them at all; bi th tiem we had left a miel of wuuds behiend us and struk th river, evrything was qieet, and we paddled oever to th toehed and hid in th cottonwoods and was saef.

Wun of thees feloes was about seventy or upwards, and had a balld hed and verry gray whiskers. He had an oeld baterd-up slouch hat on, and a greezy bloo woollen shert, and raged oeld bloo jeens briches stuft into his boot-tops, and hoem-nit galluses—no, he oenly had wun. He had an oeld long-tailed bloo jeens coet with slik bras butons flung oever his arm, and boeth of them had big, fat, ratty-luuking carpet-bags.

Th uther felo was about therty, and drest about as ornery. After brekfast we all laed off and taukt, and th ferst thing that cum out was that thees chaps didn’t noe wun anuther.

“Whut got u into trubl?” sez th baldhead to t’uther chap.

“Wel, I’d bin seling an articl to taek th tartar off th teeth—and it duz taek it off, too, and generly th enamel along with it—but I stayd about wun niet longger than I aut to, and was just in th act of slieding out when I ran across u on th trael this sied of toun, and u toeld me thay wer cuming, and begd me to help u to get off. So I toeld u I was expecting trubl mieself, and wuud scater out with u. That’s th hoel yarn—whut’s yourn?

“Wel, I’d ben a-runing’ a litl temperans revieval thar ’bout a week, and was th pet of th wimen foeks, big and litl, for I was makin’ it miety worm for th rummies, I tel u, and takin’ as much as fiev or six dolars a niet—ten sents a hed, children and niggers free—and biznes a-growin’ all th tiem, when sumhow or anuther a litl report got around last niet that I had a way of puttin’ in mi tiem with a prievet jug on th sli. A nigger rousted me out this mornin’, and toeld me th peepl was getherin’ on th qieet with thair daugs and horses, and thay’d be along prity soon and giv me ’bout haf an our’s start, and then run me doun if thay cuud; and if thay got me thay’d tar and fether me and ried me on a rael, shur. I didn’t waet for no brekfast—I worn’t hunggry.”

“Oeld man,” sed th yung wun, “I rekon we miet dubl-teem it together; whut do u think?”

“I ain’t undisposed. Whut’s yur lien—maenly?”

“Jour printer bi traed; do a litl in patent medisins; theeater-actor—trajedy, u noe; taek a tern to mezmerizm and frenolojy when thair’s a chans; teech singing-jeografy scool for a chaenj; sling a lekcher sumtiems—o, I do lots of things—moest enything that cums handy, so it ain’t werk. Whut’s yur lay?”

“I’ve dun considerble in th doctoring way in mi tiem. Layin’ on o’ hands is mi best holt—for canser and paralisis, and sich things; and I k’n tel a forchun prity guud when I’ve got sumbody along to fiend out th facts for me. Preachin’s mi lien, too, and workin’ camp-meetin’s, and missionaryin’ around.”

Noebody never sed enything for a whiel; then th yung man hoev a si and sez:

“Alas!”

“Whut ’re u alassin’ about?” sez th balld-hed.

“To think I shuud hav livd to be leding such a lief, and be degraeded doun into such cumpany.” And he begun to wiep th corner of his ie with a rag.

“Dern yur skin, ain’t th cumpany guud enuf for u?” sez th baldhead, prity pert and uppish.

“Yes, it is guud enuf for me; it’s as guud as I dezerv; for hoo fetched me so lo when I was so hi? I did mieself. I don’t blaem u, jentlmen—far frum it; I don’t blaem enybody. I dezerv it all. Let th coeld werld do its werst; wun thing I noe—thair’s a graev sumwhair for me. Th werld may go on just as it’s allways dun, and taek evrything frum me—luvd wuns, property, evrything; but it can’t taek that. Sum day I’ll lie doun in it and forget it all, and mi pur broeken hart wil be at rest.” He went on a-wieping.

“Drot yur por broeken hart,” sez th baldhead; “whut ar u heeving yur por broeken hart at us f’r? we hain’t dun nuthing.”

“No, I noe u haeven’t. I ain’t blaeming u, jentlmen. I braut mieself doun—yes, I did it mieself. It’s riet I shuud sufer—perfectly riet—I don’t maek eny moen.”

“Braut u doun frum whar? Whar was u braut doun frum?”

“Aa, u wuud not beleev me; th werld never beleevs—let it pas—’tis no mater. Th seecret of mi berth—”

“Th seecret of yur berth! Do u meen to say—”

“Jentlmen,” sez th yung man, verry solem, “I wil reveel it to u, for I feel I may hav confidens in u. Bi riets I am a duek!”











Jim’s ies bugd out when he herd that; and I rekon mien did, too. Then th baldhead sez: “No! u can’t meen it?”

“Yes. Mi graet-grandfaather, eldest sun of th Duek of Bridgewater, fled to this cuntry about th end of th last senchery, to breeth th puer air of freedom; marryd heer, and died, leeving a sun, his oen faather dieing about th saem tiem. Th second sun of th laet duek seezd th tietls and estaets—th infant reeal duek was ignord. I am th linial desendant of that infant—I am th rietful Duek of Bridgewater; and heer am I, forlorn, torn frum mi hi estaet, hunted of men, despiezd bi th coeld werld, raged, worn, hart-broeken, and degraeded to th companyonship of felons on a raft!”

Jim pityd him ever so much, and so did I. We tried to cumfort him, but he sed it worn’t much ues, he couldn’t be much cumforted; sed if we was a miend to aknolej him, that wuud do him mor guud than moest enything els; so we sed we wuud, if he wuud tel us how. He sed we aut to bo when we spoek to him, and say “Yur Graes,” or “Mi Lord,” or “Yur Lordship”—and he wouldn’t miend it if we calld him plaen “Bridgewater,” which, he sed, was a tietl enyway, and not a naem; and wun of us aut to waet on him at diner, and do eny litl thing for him he wonted dun.

Wel, that was all eezy, so we dun it. All thru diner Jim stuud around and waeted on him, and sez, “Wil yo’ Graes hav sum o’ dis or sum o’ dat?” and so on, and a body cuud see it was miety pleezing to him.

But th oeld man got prity sielent bi and bi—didn’t hav much to say, and didn’t luuk prity cumfortabl oever all that peting that was going on around that duek. He seemd to hav sumthing on his miend. So, along in th afternoon, he sez:

“Luuky heer, Bilgewater,” he sez, “I’m naeshun sorry for u, but u ain’t th oenly person that’s had trubls liek that.”

“No?”

“No u ain’t. U ain’t th oenly person that’s ben snaekt doun wrongfully out’n a hi plaes.”

“Alas!”

“No, u ain’t th oenly person that’s had a seecret of his berth.” And, bi jings, he begins to cri.

“Hoeld! Whut do u meen?”

“Bilgewater, kin I trust u?” sez th oeld man, stil sort of sobing.

“To th biter deth!” He tuuk th oeld man bi th hand and sqeezd it, and sez, “That seecret of yur being: speek!”

“Bilgewater, I am th laet Daufin!”











U bet u, Jim and me staird this tiem. Then th duek sez:

“U ar whut?”

“Yes, mi frend, it is too troo—yur ies is lookin’ at this verry moement on th por disapeerd Daufin, Looy th Seventeen, sun of Looy th Sixteen and Marry Antonette.”

“U! At yur aej! No! U meen u’re th laet Charlemagne; u must be six or seven hundred yeers oeld, at th verry leest.”

“Trubl has dun it, Bilgewater, trubl has dun it; trubl has brung thees gray hairs and this preematur balditude. Yes, jentlmen, u see befor u, in bloo jeens and mizery, th wanderin’, exield, trampld-on, and sufferin’ rietful King of France.”

Wel, he cried and tuuk on so that me and Jim didn’t noe hardly whut to do, we was so sorry—and so glad and proud we’d got him with us, too. So we set in, liek we dun befor with th duek, and tried to cumfort him. But he sed it worn’t no ues, nuthing but to be ded and dun with it all cuud do him eny guud; tho he sed it offen maed him feel eezyer and beter for a whiel if peepl treeted him acording to his riets, and got doun on wun nee to speek to him, and allways calld him “Yur Majesty,” and waeted on him ferst at meels, and didn’t set doun in his prezens til he askt them. So Jim and me set to majestying him, and doing this and that and t’uther for him, and standing up til he toeld us we miet set doun. This dun him heeps of guud, and so he got cheerful and cumfortabl. But th duek kiend of soured on him, and didn’t luuk a bit satisfied with th way things was going; stil, th king acted reeal frendly tords him, and sed th duek’s graet-grandfaather and all th uther Dueks of Bilgewater was a guud deel thaut of bi his faather, and was alowd to cum to th palas considerabl; but th duek stayd hufy a guud whiel, til bi and bi th king sez:

“Liek as not we got to be together a blaemd long tiem on this h-yer raft, Bilgewater, and so whut’s th ues o’ yur bein’ sour? It ’ll oenly maek things oncomfortable. It ain’t mi fallt I worn’t born a duek, it ain’t yur fallt u worn’t born a king—so whut’s th ues to wery? Maek th best o’ things th way u fiend ’em, sez I—that’s mi moto. This ain’t no bad thing that we’ve struk heer—plenty grub and an eezy lief—cum, giv us yur hand, duek, and le’s all be frends.”

Th duek dun it, and Jim and me was prity glad to see it. It tuuk away all th uncomfortableness and we felt miety guud oever it, becauz it wuud a bin a mizerabl biznes to hav eny unfriendliness on th raft; for whut u wont, abuv all things, on a raft, is for evrybody to be satisfied, and feel riet and kiend tords th uthers.

It didn’t taek me long to maek up mi miend that thees lieers worn’t no kings nor dueks at all, but just lo-doun humbugs and frauds. But I never sed nuthing, never let on; kept it to mieself; it’s th best way; then u don’t hav no qorrels, and don’t get into no trubl. If thay wonted us to call them kings and dueks, I hadn’t no objecshuns, ’long as it wuud keep pees in th family; and it worn’t no ues to tel Jim, so I didn’t tel him. If I never learnt nuthing els out of pap, I learnt that th best way to get along with his kiend of peepl is to let them hav thair oen way.




















CHAPTER XX.

Thay askt us considerabl meny qeschuns; wonted to noe whut we cuverd up th raft that way for, and laed bi in th daytiem insted of runing—was Jim a runaway nigger? Sez I:

“Guudnes sakes! wuud a runaway nigger run south?”

No, thay alowd he wouldn’t. I had to acount for things sum way, so I sez:

“Mi foeks was living in Piek County, in Missouri, whair I was born, and thay all died off but me and paa and mi bruther Ike. Paa, he ’lowed he’d braek up and go doun and liv with Unkl Ben, hoo’s got a litl wun-hors plaes on th river, forty-foer miel belo Orleans. Paa was prity pur, and had sum dets; so when he’d sqaird up thair worn’t nuthing left but sixteen dolars and our nigger, Jim. That worn’t enuf to taek us forteen hundred miel, dek pasej nor no uther way. Wel, when th river roez paa had a streek of luk wun day; he ketched this pees of a raft; so we rekond we’d go doun to Orleans on it. Paa’s luk didn’t hoeld out; a steemboet run oever th forrard corner of th raft wun niet, and we all went oeverbord and doev under th wheel; Jim and me cum up all riet, but paa was drunk, and Ike was oenly foer yeers oeld, so thay never cum up no mor. Wel, for th next day or too we had considerabl trubl, becauz peepl was allways cuming out in skifs and trieing to taek Jim away frum me, saying thay beleevd he was a runaway nigger. We don’t run daytimes no mor now; niets thay don’t bother us.”

Th duek sez:

“Leev me aloen to siefer out a way so we can run in th daytiem if we wont to. I’ll think th thing oever—I’ll invent a plan that’ll fix it. We’ll let it aloen for to-day, becauz of cors we don’t wont to go bi that toun yonder in dayliet—it mightn’t be helthy.”

Tords niet it begun to darken up and luuk liek raen; th heet lietning was sqerting around lo doun in th skie, and th leevs was begining to shiver—it was going to be prity ugly, it was eezy to see that. So th duek and th king went to oeverhalling our wigwom, to see whut th beds was liek. Mi bed was a straw tik beter than Jim’s, which was a corn-shuck tik; thair’s allways cobs around about in a shuck tik, and thay poek into u and hert; and when u roel oever th dri shuks sound liek u was roeling oever in a piel of ded leevs; it maeks such a rusling that u waek up. Wel, th duek alowd he wuud taek mi bed; but th king alowd he wouldn’t. He sez:

“I shuud a rekond th diferens in rank wuud a sejested to u that a corn-shuck bed worn’t just fitten for me to sleep on. Yur Graes ’ll taek th shuck bed yurself.”

Jim and me was in a swet agen for a minit, being afraed thair was going to be sum mor trubl amungst them; so we was prity glad when th duek sez:

“’Tis mi faet to be allways ground into th mier under th ieern heel of opreshun. Misforchen has broeken mi wuns hauty spirit; I yeeld, I submit; ’tis mi faet. I am aloen in th werld—let me sufer; can bair it.”

We got away as soon as it was guud and dark. Th king toeld us to stand wel out tords th midl of th river, and not sho a liet til we got a long ways belo th toun. We cum in siet of th litl bunch of liets bi and bi—that was th toun, u noe—and slid bi, about a haf a miel out, all riet. When we was three-qorters of a miel belo we hoisted up our signal lantern; and about ten o’clok it cum on to raen and blo and thunder and lighten liek evrything; so th king toeld us to boeth stay on woch til th wether got beter; then him and th duek cralld into th wigwom and ternd in for th niet. It was mi woch belo til twelv, but I wouldn’t a ternd in enyway if I’d had a bed, becauz a body don’t see such a storm as that evry day in th week, not bi a long siet. Mi soels, how th wiend did screem along! And evry second or too thair’d cum a glair that lit up th whiet-caps for a haf a miel around, and u’d see th ielands luuking dusty thru th raen, and th trees thrashing around in th wiend; then cums a H-Whak!—bum! bum! bumbl-umble-um-bum-bum-bum-bum—and th thunder wuud go rumbling and grumbling away, and qit—and then RIP cums anuther flash and anuther sockdolager. Th waevs moest wosht me off th raft sumtiems, but I hadn’t eny cloeths on, and didn’t miend. We didn’t hav no trubl about snags; th lietning was glairing and flittering around so constant that we cuud see them plenty soon enuf to thro her hed this way or that and mis them.

I had th midl woch, u noe, but I was prity sleepy bi that tiem, so Jim he sed he wuud stand th ferst haf of it for me; he was allways miety guud that way, Jim was. I cralld into th wigwom, but th king and th duek had thair legs spralld around so thair worn’t no sho for me; so I laed outsied—I didn’t miend th raen, becauz it was worm, and th waevs worn’t runing so hi now. About too thay cum up agen, tho, and Jim was going to call me; but he chaenjd his miend, becauz he rekond thay worn’t hi enuf yet to do eny harm; but he was mistaeken about that, for prity soon all of a suden along cums a reguelar ripper and wosht me oeverbord. It moest kild Jim a-lafing. He was th eezyest nigger to laf that ever was, enyway.

I tuuk th woch, and Jim he laed doun and snored away; and bi and bi th storm let up for guud and all; and th ferst cabin-liet that shoed I rousted him out, and we slid th raft into hieding qorters for th day.

Th king got out an oeld ratty dek of cards after brekfast, and him and th duek playd seven-up a whiel, fiev sents a gaem. Then thay got tierd of it, and alowd thay wuud “lay out a campaen,” as thay calld it. Th duek went doun into his carpet-bag, and fetched up a lot of litl printed bils and red them out loud. Wun bil sed, “Th selebraeted Dr. Armand de Montalban, of Paris,” wuud “lekcher on th Sieens of Frenolojy” at such and such a plaes, on th blank day of blank, at ten sents admishun, and “fernish charts of carracter at twenty-fiev sents apees.” Th duek sed that was him. In anuther bil he was th “werld-renound Shakespearian trajeedian, Garrick th Yungger, of Drury Laen, London.” In uther bils he had a lot of uther naems and dun uther wunderful things, liek fiending wauter and goeld with a “diviening-rod,” “disipaeting wich spels,” and so on. Bi and bi he sez:

“But th histrionic muez is th darling. Hav u ever trod th bords, Royalty?”

“No,” sez th king.

“U shal, then, befor u’re three days oelder, Fallen Granjer,” sez th duek. “Th ferst guud toun we cum to we’ll hier a hall and do th sord fiet in Richard III. and th balcony seen in Romeo and Juliet. How duz that striek u?”
