So when we see th raft was gon and we flat broek, thair worn’t enything for it but to tri th Royal Nonesuch anuther shaek. And I’ve pegd along ever sinss, dri as a pouder-horn. Whair’s that ten sents? Giv it heer.”











I had considerabl muny, so I giv him ten sents, but begd him to spend it for sumthing to eet, and giv me sum, becauz it was all th muny I had, and I hadn’t had nuthing to eet sinss yesterday. He never sed nuthing. Th next minit he wherls on me and sez:

“Do u rekon that nigger wuud blo on us? We’d skin him if he dun that!”

“How can he blo? Hain’t he run off?”

“No! That oeld fool soeld him, and never divieded with me, and th muny’s gon.”

“Soeld him?” I sez, and begun to cri; “whi, he was mi nigger, and that was mi muny. Whair is he?—I wont mi nigger.”

“Wel, u can’t get yur nigger, that’s all—so dri up yur blubbering. Luuky heer—do u think u’d vencher to blo on us? Blaemd if I think I’d trust u. Whi, if u was to blo on us—”

He stopt, but I never see th duek luuk so ugly out of his ies befor. I went on a-whimpering, and sez:

“I don’t wont to blo on noebody; and I ain’t got no tiem to blo, nohow. I got to tern out and fiend mi nigger.”

He luukt kiender botherd, and stuud thair with his bils flutering on his arm, thinking, and rinkling up his forhed. At last he sez:

“I’ll tel u sumthing. We got to be heer three days. If u’ll promis u wun’t blo, and wun’t let th nigger blo, I’ll tel u whair to fiend him.”

So I promist, and he sez:

“A farmer bi th naem of Silas Ph—” and then he stopt. U see, he started to tel me th trooth; but when he stopt that way, and begun to study and think agen, I rekond he was chaenjing his miend. And so he was. He wouldn’t trust me; he wonted to maek shur of having me out of th way th hoel three days. So prity soon he sez:

“Th man that baut him is naemd Abram Foster—Abram G. Foster—and he lievs forty miel bak heer in th cuntry, on th roed to Lafayette.”

“All riet,” I sez, “I can wauk it in three days. And I’ll start this verry afternoon.”

“No u wont, u’ll start now; and don’t u looz eny tiem about it, neether, nor do eny gabling bi th way. Just keep a tiet tung in yur hed and moov riet along, and then u wun’t get into trubl with us, d’ye heer?”

That was th order I wonted, and that was th wun I playd for. I wonted to be left free to werk mi plans.

“So cleer out,” he sez; “and u can tel Mr. Foster whutever u wont to. Maybe u can get him to beleev that Jim is yur nigger—sum idiots don’t reqier docuements—leastways I’ve herd thair’s such doun South heer. And when u tel him th handbil and th reword’s boegus, maybe he’ll beleev u when u explaen to him whut th iedeea was for geting ’em out. Go ’long now, and tel him enything u wont to; but miend u don’t werk yur jaw eny between heer and thair.”











So I left, and struk for th bak cuntry. I didn’t luuk around, but I kiender felt liek he was woching me. But I knowed I cuud tier him out at that. I went straet out in th cuntry as much as a miel befor I stopt; then I dubld bak thru th wuuds tords Phelps’. I rekond I beter start in on mi plan straet off without fooling around, becauz I wonted to stop Jim’s mouth til thees feloes cuud get away. I didn’t wont no trubl with thair kiend. I’d seen all I wonted to of them, and wonted to get entierly shut of them.














CHAPTER XXXII.

When I got thair it was all stil and Sunday-liek, and hot and sunshieny; th hands was gon to th feelds; and thair was them kiend of faent dronings of bugs and flies in th air that maeks it seem so loensum and liek evrybody’s ded and gon; and if a breez fans along and qivers th leevs it maeks u feel mornful, becauz u feel liek it’s spirits whispering—spirits that’s bin ded ever so meny yeers—and u allways think thay’re tauking about u. As a jeneral thing it maeks a body wish he was ded, too, and dun with it all.

Phelps’ was wun of thees litl wun-hors coton plantaeshuns, and thay all luuk aliek. A rael fens round a too-aeker yard; a stiel maed out of logs sawd off and up-ended in steps, liek barrels of a diferent length, to cliem oever th fens with, and for th wimen to stand on when thay ar going to jump on to a hors; sum sikly gras-paches in th big yard, but moestly it was bair and smooth, liek an oeld hat with th nap rubd off; big dubl log-hous for th whiet foeks—hued logs, with th chinks stopt up with mud or mortar, and thees mud-strieps bin whietwosht sum tiem or anuther; round-log kichen, with a big braud, oepen but rooft pasej joining it to th hous; log smoek-hous bak of th kichen; three litl log nigger-cabins in a ro t’uther sied th smoek-hous; wun litl hut all bi itself away doun agenst th bak fens, and sum outbildings doun a pees th uther sied; ash-hoper and big ketl to biel soep in bi th litl hut; bench bi th kichen dor, with buket of wauter and a gord; hound asleep thair in th sun; mor hounds asleep round about; about three shaed trees away off in a corner; sum curant buushes and goosberry buushes in wun plaes bi th fens; outsied of th fens a garden and a wautermelon pach; then th coton feelds begins, and after th feelds th wuuds.

I went around and clumb oever th bak stiel bi th ash-hoper, and started for th kichen. When I got a litl ways I herd th dim hum of a spining-wheel waeling along up and sinking along doun agen; and then I knowed for serten I wisht I was ded—for that is th lonesomest sound in th hoel werld.

I went riet along, not fixing up eny particuelar plan, but just trusting to Providens to puut th riet werds in mi mouth when th tiem cum; for I’d noetist that Providens allways did puut th riet werds in mi mouth if I left it aloen.

When I got haf-way, ferst wun hound and then anuther got up and went for me, and of cors I stopt and faest them, and kept stil. And such anuther powwow as thay maed! In a qorter of a minit I was a kiend of a hub of a wheel, as u may say—spoeks maed out of daugs—sercl of fifteen of them pakt together around me, with thair neks and noezes strecht up tords me, a-barking and houling; and mor a-cuming; u cuud see them saeling oever fenses and around corners frum everywheres.

A nigger wuuman cum tairing out of th kichen with a roeling-pin in her hand, singing out, “Begone u Tige! u Spot! begone sah!” and she fetched ferst wun and then anuther of them a clip and sent them houling, and then th rest foloed; and th next second haf of them cum bak, waging thair taels around me, and maeking frends with me. Thair ain’t no harm in a hound, nohow.

And behiend th wuuman cums a litl nigger gerl and too litl nigger boys without enything on but toe-linen sherts, and thay hung on to thair muther’s goun, and peeped out frum behiend her at me, bashful, th way thay allways do. And heer cums th whiet wuuman runing frum th hous, about forty-fiev or fifty yeer oeld, bairheded, and her spining-stik in her hand; and behiend her cums her litl whiet children, acting th saem way th litl niggers was doing. She was smieling all oever so she cuud hardly stand—and sez:

“It’s u, at last!—ain’t it?”

I out with a “Yes’m” befor I thaut.











She grabd me and hugd me tiet; and then gript me bi boeth hands and shuuk and shuuk; and th teers cum in her ies, and run doun oever; and she couldn’t seem to hug and shaek enuf, and kept saying, “U don’t luuk as much liek yur muther as I rekond u wuud; but law sakes, I don’t cair for that, I’m so glad to see u! Deer, deer, it duz seem liek I cuud eet u up! Children, it’s yur cuzin Tom!—tel him houdy.”

But thay dukt thair heds, and puut thair finggers in thair mouths, and hid behiend her. So she run on:

“Lize, hery up and get him a hot brekfast riet away—or did u get yur brekfast on th boet?”

I sed I had got it on th boet. So then she started for th hous, leding me bi th hand, and th children taging after. When we got thair she set me doun in a split-bottomed chair, and set herself doun on a litl lo stool in frunt of me, hoelding boeth of mi hands, and sez:

“Now I can hav a guud luuk at u; and, laws-a-me, I’ve bin hunggry for it a meny and a meny a tiem, all thees long yeers, and it’s cum at last! We bin expecting u a cupl of days and mor. Whut kep’ u?—boet get aground?”

“Yes’m—she—”

“Don’t say yes’m—say Ant Sally. Whair’d she get aground?”

I didn’t rietly noe whut to say, becauz I didn’t noe whether th boet wuud be cuming up th river or doun. But I go a guud deel on instinkt; and mi instinkt sed she wuud be cuming up—frum doun tords Orleans. That didn’t help me much, tho; for I didn’t noe th naems of bars doun that way. I see I’d got to invent a bar, or forget th naem of th wun we got aground on—or—Now I struk an iedeea, and fetched it out:

“It worn’t th grounding—that didn’t keep us bak but a litl. We blowed out a silinder-hed.”

“Guud graeshus! enybody hert?”

“No’m. Kild a nigger.”

“Wel, it’s luky; becauz sumtiems peepl do get hert. Too yeers ago last Christmas yur unkl Silas was cuming up frum Newrleans on th oeld Lally Ruuk, and she blowed out a silinder-hed and cripld a man. And I think he died afterwards. He was a Baptist. Yur unkl Silas knowed a family in Baton Roozh that knowed his peepl verry wel. Yes, I remember now, he did die. Mortificaeshun set in, and thay had to ampuetaet him. But it didn’t saev him. Yes, it was mortificaeshun—that was it. He ternd bloo all oever, and died in th hoep of a glorius rezerecshun. Thay say he was a siet to luuk at. Yur unkl’s bin up to th toun evry day to fech u. And he’s gon agen, not mor’n an our ago; he’ll be bak eny minit now. U must a met him on th roed, didn’t u?—oeldish man, with a—”

“No, I didn’t see noebody, Ant Sally. Th boet landed just at dayliet, and I left mi bagej on th whorf-boet and went luuking around th toun and out a pees in th cuntry, to puut in th tiem and not get heer too soon; and so I cum doun th bak way.”

“Hoo’d u giv th bagej to?”

“Noebody.”

“Whi, chield, it ’ll be stoel!”

“Not whair I hid it I rekon it wun’t,” I sez.

“How’d u get yur brekfast so erly on th boet?”

It was kiender thin ies, but I sez:

“Th capten see me standing around, and toeld me I beter hav sumthing to eet befor I went ashor; so he tuuk me in th texas to th offisers’ lunch, and giv me all I wonted.”

I was geting so uneezy I couldn’t lisen guud. I had mi miend on th children all th tiem; I wonted to get them out to wun sied and pump them a litl, and fiend out hoo I was. But I couldn’t get no sho, Mrs. Phelps kept it up and run on so. Prity soon she maed th coeld chils streek all doun mi bak, becauz she sez:

“But heer we’re a-runing on this way, and u hain’t toeld me a werd about Sis, nor eny of them. Now I’ll rest mi werks a litl, and u start up yourn; just tel me evrything—tel me all about ’m all evry wun of ’m; and how thay ar, and whut thay’re doing, and whut thay toeld u to tel me; and evry last thing u can think of.”

Wel, I see I was up a stump—and up it guud. Providens had stuud bi me this fer all riet, but I was hard and tiet aground now. I see it worn’t a bit of ues to tri to go ahed—I’d got to thro up mi hand. So I sez to mieself, heer’s anuther plaes whair I got to resk th trooth. I oepend mi mouth to begin; but she grabd me and husld me in behiend th bed, and sez:

“Heer he cums! Stik yur hed doun loeer—thair, that’ll do; u can’t be seen now. Don’t u let on u’re heer. I’ll play a joek on him. Children, don’t u say a werd.”

I see I was in a fix now. But it worn’t no ues to wery; thair worn’t nuthing to do but just hoeld stil, and tri and be redy to stand frum under when th lietning struk.

I had just wun litl glimps of th oeld jentlman when he cum in; then th bed hid him. Mrs. Phelps she jumps for him, and sez:

“Has he cum?”

“No,” sez her huzband.

“Guud-ness graeshus!” she sez, “whut in th warld can hav becum of him?”

“I can’t imajin,” sez th oeld jentlman; “and I must say it maeks me dredful uneezy.”

“Uneezy!” she sez; “I’m redy to go distracted! He must a cum; and u’ve mist him along th roed. I noe it’s so—sumthing tels me so.”

“Whi, Sally, I couldn’t mis him along th roed—u noe that.”

“But o, deer, deer, whut wil Sis say! He must a cum! U must a mist him. He—”

“O, don’t distres me eny mor’n I’m allredy distrest. I don’t noe whut in th werld to maek of it. I’m at mi wit’s end, and I don’t miend aknolejing ’t I’m riet doun scaird. But thair’s no hoep that he’s cum; for he couldn’t cum and me mis him. Sally, it’s terribl—just terribl—sumthing’s hapend to th boet, shur!”

“Whi, Silas! Luuk yonder!—up th roed!—ain’t that sumbody cuming?”

He sprung to th windo at th hed of th bed, and that giv Mrs. Phelps th chans she wonted. She stoopt doun qik at th fuut of th bed and giv me a puul, and out I cum; and when he ternd bak frum th windo thair she stuud, a-beeming and a-smieling liek a hous afier, and I standing prity meek and swety alongsied. Th oeld jentlman staird, and sez:

“Whi, hoo’s that?”

“Hoo do u rekon ’t is?”











“I hain’t no iedeea. Hoo is it?”

“It’s Tom Sawyer!”

Bi jings, I moest slumpt thru th flor! But thair worn’t no tiem to swop nievs; th oeld man grabd me bi th hand and shuuk, and kept on shaeking; and all th tiem how th wuuman did dans around and laf and cri; and then how thay boeth did fier off qeschuns about Sid, and Mary, and th rest of th trieb.

But if thay was joyful, it worn’t nuthing to whut I was; for it was liek being born agen, I was so glad to fiend out hoo I was. Wel, thay froez to me for too ours; and at last, when mi chin was so tierd it couldn’t hardly go eny mor, I had toeld them mor about mi family—I meen th Sawyer family—than ever hapend to eny six Sawyer familys. And I explaend all about how we blowed out a silinder-hed at th mouth of Whiet River, and it tuuk us three days to fix it. Which was all riet, and werkt ferst-raet; becauz thay didn’t noe but whut it wuud taek three days to fix it. If I’d a calld it a bolthead it wuud a dun just as wel.

Now I was feeling prity cumfortabl all doun wun sied, and prity uncumfortabl all up th uther. Being Tom Sawyer was eezy and cumfortabl, and it stayd eezy and cumfortabl til bi and bi I heer a steemboet caufing along doun th river. Then I sez to mieself, s’poez Tom Sawyer cums doun on that boet? And s’poez he steps in heer eny minit, and sings out mi naem befor I can thro him a wink to keep qieet?

Wel, I couldn’t hav it that way; it wouldn’t do at all. I must go up th roed and waylay him. So I toeld th foeks I rekond I wuud go up to th toun and fech doun mi bagej. Th oeld jentlman was for going along with me, but I sed no, I cuud driev th hors mieself, and I druther he wouldn’t taek no trubl about me.














CHAPTER XXXIII.

So I started for toun in th wagon, and when I was haf-way I see a wagon cuming, and shur enuf it was Tom Sawyer, and I stopt and waeted til he cum along. I sez “Hoeld on!” and it stopt alongsied, and his mouth oepend up liek a trunk, and stayd so; and he swoloed too or three tiems liek a person that’s got a dri throet, and then sez:

“I hain’t ever dun u no harm. U noe that. So, then, whut u wont to cum bak and haa’nt me for?”

I sez:

“I hain’t cum bak—I hain’t bin gon.”

When he herd mi vois it righted him up sum, but he worn’t qiet satisfied yet. He sez:

“Don’t u play nuthing on me, becauz I wouldn’t on u. Onest injun now, u ain’t a goest?”

“Onest injun, I ain’t,” I sez.

“Wel—I—I—wel, that aut to setl it, of cors; but I can’t sumhow seem to understand it no way. Luuky heer, worn’t u ever merderd at all?”

“No. I worn’t ever merderd at all—I playd it on them. U cum in heer and feel of me if u don’t beleev me.”

So he dun it; and it satisfied him; and he was that glad to see me agen he didn’t noe whut to do. And he wonted to noe all about it riet off, becauz it was a grand advencher, and misteerius, and so it hit him whair he livd. But I sed, leev it aloen til bi and bi; and toeld his driever to waet, and we droev off a litl pees, and I toeld him th kiend of a fix I was in, and whut did he rekon we beter do? He sed, let him aloen a minit, and don’t disterb him. So he thaut and thaut, and prity soon he sez:

“It’s all riet; I’ve got it. Taek mi trunk in yur wagon, and let on it’s yur’n; and u tern bak and fool along slo, so as to get to th hous about th tiem u aut to; and I’ll go tords toun a pees, and taek a fresh start, and get thair a qorter or a haf an our after u; and u needn’t let on to noe me at ferst.”

I sez:

“All riet; but waet a minit. Thair’s wun mor thing—a thing that noebody don’t noe but me. And that is, thair’s a nigger heer that I’m a-trieing to steel out of slaevery, and his naem is Jim—oeld Mis Watson’s Jim.”

He sez:

“Whut! Whi, Jim is—”

He stopt and went to studying. I sez:

“I noe whut u’ll say. U’ll say it’s derty, lo-doun biznes; but whut if it is? I’m lo doun; and I’m a-going to steel him, and I wont u keep mum and not let on. Wil u?”

His ie lit up, and he sez:

“I’ll help u steel him!”

Wel, I let go all holts then, liek I was shot. It was th moest astonishing speech I ever herd—and I’m bound to say Tom Sawyer fel considerabl in mi estimaeshun. Oenly I couldn’t beleev it. Tom Sawyer a nigger-steeler!

“O, shuks!” I sez; “u’re joeking.”

“I ain’t joeking, eether.”

“Wel, then,” I sez, “joeking or no joeking, if u heer enything sed about a runaway nigger, don’t forget to remember that u don’t noe nuthing about him, and I don’t noe nuthing about him.”

Then we tuuk th trunk and puut it in mi wagon, and he droev off his way and I droev mien. But of cors I forgot all about drieving slo on acounts of being glad and fuul of thinking; so I got hoem a heep too qik for that length of a trip. Th oeld jentlman was at th dor, and he sez:

“Whi, this is wunderful! Hooever wuud a thaut it was in that mair to do it? I wish we’d a tiemd her. And she hain’t sweted a hair—not a hair. It’s wunderful. Whi, I wouldn’t taek a hundred dolars for that hors now—I wouldn’t, onest; and yet I’d a soeld her for fifteen befor, and thaut ’twas all she was werth.”

That’s all he sed. He was th innocentest, best oeld soel I ever see. But it worn’t serpriezing; becauz he worn’t oenly just a farmer, he was a preecher, too, and had a litl wun-hors log cherch doun bak of th plantaeshun, which he bilt it himself at his oen expens, for a cherch and scoolhous, and never charjd nuthing for his preeching, and it was werth it, too. Thair was plenty uther farmer-preechers liek that, and dun th saem way, doun South.

In about haf an our Tom’s wagon droev up to th frunt stiel, and Ant Sally she see it thru th windo, becauz it was oenly about fifty yards, and sez:

“Whi, thair’s sumbody cum! I wunder hoo ’tis? Whi, I do beleev it’s a straenjer. Jimy” (that’s wun of th children) “run and tel Lize to puut on anuther plaet for diner.”

Evrybody maed a rush for th frunt dor, becauz, of cors, a straenjer don’t cum evry yeer, and so he lays oever th yaller-feever, for interest, when he duz cum. Tom was oever th stiel and starting for th hous; th wagon was spining up th roed for th vilej, and we was all buncht in th frunt dor. Tom had his stor cloeths on, and an audyens—and that was allways nuts for Tom Sawyer. In them sercumstanses it worn’t no trubl to him to thro in an amount of stiel that was sootabl. He worn’t a boy to meeky along up that yard liek a sheep; no, he cum ca’m and important, liek th ram. When he got a-frunt of us he lifts his hat ever so graeshus and daenty, liek it was th lid of a box that had buterflies asleep in it and he didn’t wont to disterb them, and sez:

“Mr. Archibald Nichols, I prezoom?”











“No, mi boy,” sez th oeld jentlman, “I’m sorry to say ’t yur driever has deseevd u; Nichols’s plaes is doun a mater of three miel mor. Cum in, cum in.”

Tom he tuuk a luuk bak oever his shoelder, and sez, “Too laet—he’s out of siet.”

“Yes, he’s gon, mi sun, and u must cum in and eet yur diner with us; and then we’ll hich up and taek u doun to Nichols’s.”

“O, I can’t maek u so much trubl; I couldn’t think of it. I’ll wauk—I don’t miend th distans.”

“But we wun’t let u wauk—it wouldn’t be Suthern hospitality to do it. Cum riet in.”

“O, do,” sez Ant Sally; “it ain’t a bit of trubl to us, not a bit in th werld. U must stay. It’s a long, dusty three miel, and we can’t let u wauk. And, besieds, I’ve allredy toeld ’em to puut on anuther plaet when I see u cuming; so u mustn’t disapoint us. Cum riet in and maek yurself at hoem.”

So Tom he thankt them verry harty and hansum, and let himself be perswaeded, and cum in; and when he was in he sed he was a straenjer frum Hicksville, Ohio, and his naem was William Thompson—and he maed anuther bo.

Wel, he run on, and on, and on, maeking up stuf about Hicksville and evrybody in it he cuud invent, and I geting a litl nervious, and wundering how this was going to help me out of mi scraep; and at last, stil tauking along, he reecht oever and kist Ant Sally riet on th mouth, and then setld bak agen in his chair cumfortabl, and was going on tauking; but she jumpt up and wiept it off with th bak of her hand, and sez:

“U owdacious pupy!”

He luukt kiend of hert, and sez:

“I’m serpriezd at u, m’am.”

“U’re s’rp—Whi, whut do u rekon I am? I’ve a guud noeshun to taek and—Say, whut do u meen bi kising me?”

He luukt kiend of humbl, and sez:

“I didn’t meen nuthing, m’am. I didn’t meen no harm. I—I—thaut u’d liek it.”

“Whi, u born fool!” She tuuk up th spining stik, and it luukt liek it was all she cuud do to keep frum giving him a crak with it. “Whut maed u think I’d liek it?”

“Wel, I don’t noe. Oenly, thay—thay—toeld me u wuud.”

“Thay toeld u I wuud. Hooever toeld u’s anuther loonatic. I never herd th beet of it. Hoo’s thay?”

“Whi, evrybody. Thay all sed so, m’am.”

It was all she cuud do to hoeld in; and her ies snapt, and her finggers werkt liek she wonted to scrach him; and she sez:

“Hoo’s ‘evrybody’? Out with thair naems, or ther’ll be an idiot short.”

He got up and luukt distrest, and fumbld his hat, and sez:

“I’m sorry, and I worn’t expecting it. Thay toeld me to. Thay all toeld me to. Thay all sed, kis her; and sed she’d liek it. Thay all sed it—evry wun of them. But I’m sorry, m’am, and I wun’t do it no mor—I wun’t, onest.”

“U wun’t, wun’t u? Wel, I sh’d rekon u wun’t!”

“No’m, I’m onest about it; I wun’t ever do it agen—til u ask me.”

“Til I ask u! Wel, I never see th beet of it in mi born days! I lay u’ll be th Methusalem-numskul of creaeshun befor ever I ask u—or th lieks of u.”

“Wel,” he sez, “it duz serpriez me so. I can’t maek it out, sumhow. Thay sed u wuud, and I thaut u wuud. But—” He stopt and luukt around slo, liek he wisht he cuud run across a frendly ie somewheres, and fetched up on th oeld jentlman’s, and sez, “Didn’t u think she’d liek me to kis her, ser?”

“Whi, no; I—I—wel, no, I b’lieve I didn’t.”

Then he luuks on around th saem way to me, and sez:

“Tom, didn’t u think Ant Sally ’d oepen out her arms and say, ‘Sid Sawyer—‘”

“Mi land!” she sez, braeking in and jumping for him, “u impuedent yung rascal, to fool a body so—” and was going to hug him, but he fended her off, and sez:

“No, not til u’ve askt me ferst.”

So she didn’t looz no tiem, but askt him; and hugd him and kist him oever and oever agen, and then ternd him oever to th oeld man, and he tuuk whut was left. And after thay got a litl qieet agen she sez:

“Whi, deer me, I never see such a serpriez. We worn’t luuking for u at all, but oenly Tom. Sis never roet to me about enybody cuming but him.”

“It’s becauz it worn’t intended for eny of us to cum but Tom,” he sez; “but I begd and begd, and at th last minit she let me cum, too; so, cuming doun th river, me and Tom thaut it wuud be a ferst-raet serpriez for him to cum heer to th hous ferst, and for me to bi and bi tag along and drop in, and let on to be a straenjer. But it was a mistaek, Ant Sally. This ain’t no helthy plaes for a straenjer to cum.”

“No—not impuedent whelps, Sid. U aut to had yur jaws boxt; I hain’t bin so puut out sinss I don’t noe when. But I don’t cair, I don’t miend th terms—I’d be wiling to stand a thouzand such joeks to hav u heer. Wel, to think of that performans! I don’t deni it, I was moest putrified with astonishment when u giv me that smak.”











We had diner out in that braud oepen pasej betwixt th hous and th kichen; and thair was things enuf on that taebl for seven familys—and all hot, too; nun of yur flaby, tuf meet that’s laed in a cubord in a damp selar all niet and taests liek a hunk of oeld coeld canibal in th morning. Unkl Silas he askt a prity long blesing oever it, but it was werth it; and it didn’t cool it a bit, neether, th way I’ve seen them kiend of interupshuns do lots of tiems. Thair was a considerabl guud deel of tauk all th afternoon, and me and Tom was on th luukout all th tiem; but it worn’t no ues, thay didn’t hapen to say nuthing about eny runaway nigger, and we was afraed to tri to werk up to it. But at super, at niet, wun of th litl boys sez:

“Paa, mayn’t Tom and Sid and me go to th sho?”

“No,” sez th oeld man, “I rekon thair ain’t going to be eny; and u couldn’t go if thair was; becauz th runaway nigger toeld Burton and me all about that scandalus sho, and Burton sed he wuud tel th peepl; so I rekon thay’ve droev th owdacious loefers out of toun befor this tiem.”

So thair it was!—but I couldn’t help it. Tom and me was to sleep in th saem room and bed; so, being tierd, we bid guud-niet and went up to bed riet after super, and clumb out of th windo and doun th lietning-rod, and shuvd for th toun; for I didn’t beleev enybody was going to giv th king and th duek a hint, and so if I didn’t hery up and giv them wun thay’d get into trubl shur.

On th roed Tom he toeld me all about how it was rekond I was merderd, and how pap disapeerd prity soon, and didn’t cum bak no mor, and whut a ster thair was when Jim run away; and I toeld Tom all about our Royal Nonesuch rapscallions, and as much of th raft voyej as I had tiem to; and as we struk into th toun and up thru th th midl of it--it was as much as haf-after aet, then—heer cums a raejing rush of peepl with torches, and an awful hooping and yeling, and banging tin pans and bloeing horns; and we jumpt to wun sied to let them go bi; and as thay went bi I see thay had th king and th duek astraddle of a rael—that is, I knowed it was th king and th duek, tho thay was all oever tar and fethers, and didn’t luuk liek nuthing in th werld that was hueman—just luukt liek a cupl of monstrus big soeljer-plumes. Wel, it maed me sik to see it; and I was sorry for them pur pityful rascals, it seemd liek I couldn’t ever feel eny hardnes agenst them eny mor in th werld. It was a dredful thing to see. Hueman beings can be awful crooel to wun anuther.











We see we was too laet—couldn’t do no guud. We askt sum straglers about it, and thay sed evrybody went to th sho luuking verry inosent; and laed lo and kept dark til th pur oeld king was in th midl of his cavortings on th staej; then sumbody giv a signal, and th hous roez up and went for them.

So we poekt along bak hoem, and I worn’t feeling so brash as I was befor, but kiend of ornery, and humbl, and to blaem, sumhow—tho I hadn’t dun nuthing. But that’s allways th way; it don’t maek no diferens whether u do riet or rong, a person’s conshens ain’t got no sens, and just goes for him enyway. If I had a yaller daug that didn’t noe no mor than a person’s conshens duz I wuud pison him. It taeks up mor room than all th rest of a person’s insieds, and yet ain’t no guud, nohow. Tom Sawyer he sez th saem.














CHAPTER XXXIV.

We stopt tauking, and got to thinking. Bi and bi Tom sez:

“Luuky heer, Huck, whut fools we ar to not think of it befor! I bet I noe whair Jim is.”

“No! Whair?”

“In that hut doun bi th ash-hoper. Whi, luuky heer. When we was at diner, didn’t u see a nigger man go in thair with sum vittles?”

“Yes.”

“Whut did u think th vittles was for?”

“For a daug.”

“So ’d I. Wel, it wasn’t for a daug.”

“Whi?”

“Becauz part of it was wautermelon.”

“So it was—I noetist it. Wel, it duz beet all that I never thaut about a daug not eeting wautermelon. It shoes how a body can see and don’t see at th saem tiem.”

“Wel, th nigger unlokt th padlok when he went in, and he lokt it agen when he caem out. He fetched unkl a kee about th tiem we got up frum taebl—saem kee, I bet. Wautermelon shoes man, lok shoes prizoner; and it ain’t liekly thair’s too prizoners on such a litl plantaeshun, and whair th peepl’s all so kiend and guud. Jim’s th prizoner. All riet—I’m glad we found it out detectiv fashun; I wouldn’t giv shuks for eny uther way. Now u werk yur miend, and study out a plan to steel Jim, and I wil study out wun, too; and we’ll taek th wun we liek th best.”

Whut a hed for just a boy to hav! If I had Tom Sawyer’s hed I wouldn’t traed it off to be a duek, nor maet of a steemboet, nor cloun in a sercus, nor nuthing I can think of. I went to thinking out a plan, but oenly just to be doing sumthing; I knowed verry wel whair th riet plan was going to cum frum. Prity soon Tom sez:

“Redy?”

“Yes,” I sez.

“All riet—bring it out.”

“Mi plan is this,” I sez. “We can eezy fiend out if it’s Jim in thair. Then get up mi canoo to-morro niet, and fech mi raft oever frum th ieland. Then th ferst dark niet that cums steel th kee out of th oeld man’s briches after he goes to bed, and shuv off doun th river on th raft with Jim, hieding daytimes and runing niets, th way me and Jim uezd to do befor. Wouldn’t that plan werk?”

“Werk? Whi, cert’nly it wuud werk, liek rats a-fieting. But it’s too blaem’ simpl; thair ain’t nuthing to it. Whut’s th guud of a plan that ain’t no mor trubl than that? It’s as mield as goos-milk. Whi, Huck, it wouldn’t maek no mor tauk than braeking into a soep factory.”

I never sed nuthing, becauz I worn’t expecting nuthing diferent; but I knowed miety wel that whenever he got his plan redy it wouldn’t hav nun of them objecshuns to it.

And it didn’t. He toeld me whut it was, and I see in a minit it was werth fifteen of mien for stiel, and wuud maek Jim just as free a man as mien wuud, and maybe get us all kild besieds. So I was satisfied, and sed we wuud wallts in on it. I needn’t tel whut it was heer, becauz I knowed it wouldn’t stay th way, it was. I knowed he wuud be chaenjing it around evry which way as we went along, and heeving in nue bullinesses whairever he got a chans. And that is whut he dun.

Wel, wun thing was ded shur, and that was that Tom Sawyer was in ernest, and was actuly going to help steel that nigger out of slaevery. That was th thing that was too meny for me. Heer was a boy that was respectabl and wel brung up; and had a carracter to looz; and foeks at hoem that had carracters; and he was briet and not lether-heded; and noeing and not ignorant; and not meen, but kiend; and yet heer he was, without eny mor pried, or rietnes, or feeling, than to stoop to this biznes, and maek himself a shaem, and his family a shaem, befor evrybody. I couldn’t understand it no way at all. It was outraejus, and I knowed I aut to just up and tel him so; and so be his troo frend, and let him qit th thing riet whair he was and saev himself. And I did start to tel him; but he shut me up, and sez:

“Don’t u rekon I noe whut I’m about? Don’t I generly noe whut I’m about?”

“Yes.”

“Didn’t I say I was going to help steel th nigger?”

“Yes.”

“Wel, then.”

That’s all he sed, and that’s all I sed. It worn’t no ues to say eny mor; becauz when he sed he’d do a thing, he allways dun it. But I couldn’t maek out how he was wiling to go into this thing; so I just let it go, and never botherd no mor about it. If he was bound to hav it so, I couldn’t help it.

When we got hoem th hous was all dark and stil; so we went on doun to th hut bi th ash-hoper for to examin it. We went thru th yard so as to see whut th hounds wuud do. Thay knowed us, and didn’t maek no mor noiz than cuntry daugs is allways doing when enything cums bi in th niet. When we got to th cabin we tuuk a luuk at th frunt and th too sieds; and on th sied I worn’t aqaented with—which was th north sied—we found a sqair windo-hoel, up tolerabl hi, with just wun stout bord naeld across it. I sez:

“Heer’s th tiket. This hoel’s big enuf for Jim to get thru if we rench off th bord.”

Tom sez:

“It’s as simpl as tit-tat-toe, three-in-a-ro, and as eezy as playing hooky. I shuud hoep we can fiend a way that’s a litl mor complicaeted than that, Huck Finn.”











“Wel, then,” I sez, “how ’ll it do to saw him out, th way I dun befor I was merderd that tiem?”

“That’s mor liek,” he sez. “It’s reeal misteerius, and trublsum, and guud,” he sez; “but I bet we can fiend a way that’s twies as long. Thair ain’t no hery; le’s keep on luuking around.”

Betwixt th hut and th fens, on th bak sied, was a leen-to that joind th hut at th eevs, and was maed out of plank. It was as long as th hut, but narro—oenly about six fuut wied. Th dor to it was at th south end, and was padlokt. Tom he went to th soep-ketl and sercht around, and fetched bak th ieern thing thay lift th lid with; so he tuuk it and priezd out wun of th staepls. Th chaen fel doun, and we oepend th dor and went in, and shut it, and struk a mach, and see th shed was oenly bilt agenst a cabin and hadn’t no conecshun with it; and thair worn’t no flor to th shed, nor nuthing in it but sum oeld rusty playd-out hoes and spaeds and piks and a cripld plow. Th mach went out, and so did we, and shuvd in th staepl agen, and th dor was lokt as guud as ever. Tom was joyful. He sez;

“Now we’re all riet. We’ll dig him out. It ’ll taek about a week!”

Then we started for th hous, and I went in th bak dor—u oenly hav to puul a bukskin lach-string, thay don’t fasen th dors—but that worn’t romantical enuf for Tom Sawyer; no way wuud do him but he must cliem up th lietning-rod. But after he got up haf way about three tiems, and mist fier and fel evry tiem, and th last tiem moest busted his braens out, he thaut he’d got to giv it up; but after he was rested he alowd he wuud giv her wun mor tern for luk, and this tiem he maed th trip.

In th morning we was up at braek of day, and doun to th nigger cabins to pet th daugs and maek frends with th nigger that fed Jim—if it was Jim that was being fed. Th niggers was just geting thru brekfast and starting for th feelds; and Jim’s nigger was pieling up a tin pan with bred and meet and things; and whilst th uthers was leeving, th kee cum frum th hous.

This nigger had a guud-naecherd, chukl-heded faes, and his wuul was all tied up in litl bunches with thred. That was to keep wiches off. He sed th wiches was pestering him awful thees niets, and maeking him see all kiends of straenj things, and heer all kiends of straenj werds and noizes, and he didn’t beleev he was ever witched so long befor in his lief. He got so werkt up, and got to runing on so about his trubls, he forgot all about whut he’d bin a-going to do. So Tom sez:

“Whut’s th vittles for? Going to feed th daugs?”

Th nigger kiend of smield around grajualy oever his faes, liek when u heev a brikbat in a mud-pudl, and he sez:

“Yes, Mars Sid, A daug. Cur’us daug, too. Duz u wont to go en luuk at ’im?”

“Yes.”

I huncht Tom, and whispers:

“U going, riet heer in th daybraek? that worn’t th plan.”

“No, it worn’t; but it’s th plan now.”

So, drat him, we went along, but I didn’t liek it much. When we got in we couldn’t hardly see enything, it was so dark; but Jim was thair, shur enuf, and cuud see us; and he sings out:

“Whi, Huck! En guud lan’! ain’ dat Misto Tom?”

I just knowed how it wuud be; I just expected it. I didn’t noe nuthing to do; and if I had I couldn’t a dun it, becauz that nigger busted in and sez:

“Whi, de graeshus sakes! do he noe u genlmen?”

We cuud see prity wel now. Tom he luukt at th nigger, stedy and kiend of wundering, and sez:

“Duz hoo noe us?”

“Whi, dis-yer runaway nigger.”

“I don’t rekon he duz; but whut puut that into yur hed?”

“Whut puut it dar? Didn’ he jis’ dis minit sing out liek he knowed u?”

Tom sez, in a puzld-up kiend of way:

“Wel, that’s miety cuerius. Hoo sung out? when did he sing out? whut did he sing out?” And terns to me, perfectly ca’m, and sez, “Did u heer enybody sing out?”

Of cors thair worn’t nuthing to be sed but th wun thing; so I sez:

“No; I ain’t herd noebody say nuthing.”

Then he terns to Jim, and luuks him oever liek he never see him befor, and sez:

“Did u sing out?”

“No, sah,” sez Jim; “I hain’t sed nuthing, sah.”

“Not a werd?”

“No, sah, I hain’t sed a werd.”

“Did u ever see us befor?”

“No, sah; not as I noes on.”

So Tom terns to th nigger, which was luuking wield and distrest, and sez, kiend of seveer:

“Whut do u rekon’s th mater with u, enyway? Whut maed u think sumbody sung out?”

“O, it’s de dad-blaem’ wiches, sah, en I wisht I was ded, I do. Day’s awluz at it, sah, en day do mos’ kil me, day sk’yers me so. Pleez to don’t tel noebody ’bout it sah, er oelay Mars Silas he’ll scole me; ’kase he say day ain’t no wiches. I jis’ wish to guudnes he was heah now—den whut wuud he say! I jis’ bet he couldn’ fien no way to git aroun’ it dis tiem. But it’s awluz jis’ so; peepl dat’s sot, stays sot; day wun’t luuk into noth’n’en fien it out f’r deyselves, en when u fien it out en tel um ’bout it, day doan’ b’lieve u.”











Tom giv him a diem, and sed we wouldn’t tel noebody; and toeld him to bi sum mor thred to tee up his wuul with; and then luuks at Jim, and sez:

“I wunder if Unkl Silas is going to hang this nigger. If I was to cach a nigger that was ungraetful enuf to run away, I wouldn’t giv him up, I’d hang him.” And whilst th nigger stept to th dor to luuk at th diem and biet it to see if it was guud, he whispers to Jim and sez:

“Don’t ever let on to noe us. And if u heer eny diging going on niets, it’s us; we’re going to set u free.”

Jim oenly had tiem to grab us bi th hand and sqeez it; then th nigger cum bak, and we sed we’d cum agen sum tiem if th nigger wonted us to; and he sed he wuud, mor particuelar if it was dark, becauz th wiches went for him moestly in th dark, and it was guud to hav foeks around then.














CHAPTER XXXV.

It wuud be moest an our yet til brekfast, so we left and struk doun into th wuuds; becauz Tom sed we got to hav sum liet to see how to dig bi, and a lantern maeks too much, and miet get us into trubl; whut we must hav was a lot of them roten chunks that’s calld fox-fier, and just maeks a sofft kiend of a glo when u lay them in a dark plaes. We fetched an armful and hid it in th weeds, and set doun to rest, and Tom sez, kiend of dissatisfied:

“Blaem it, this hoel thing is just as eezy and aukward as it can be. And so it maeks it so roten dificult to get up a dificult plan. Thair ain’t no wochman to be drugd—now thair aut to be a wochman. Thair ain’t eeven a daug to giv a sleeping-mixcher to. And thair’s Jim chained bi wun leg, with a ten-fuut chaen, to th leg of his bed: whi, all u got to do is to lift up th bedsted and slip off th chaen. And Unkl Silas he trusts evrybody; sends th kee to th punkin-heded nigger, and don’t send noebody to woch th nigger. Jim cuud a got out of that windo-hoel befor this, oenly thair wouldn’t be no ues trieing to travel with a ten-fuut chaen on his leg. Whi, drat it, Huck, it’s th stoopidest araenjment I ever see. U got to invent all th dificultys. Wel, we can’t help it; we got to do th best we can with th mateerials we’ve got. Enyhow, thair’s wun thing—thair’s mor onor in geting him out thru a lot of dificultys and daenjers, whair thair worn’t wun of them fernisht to u bi th peepl hoo it was thair duety to fernish them, and u had to contriev them all out of yur oen hed. Now luuk at just that wun thing of th lantern. When u cum doun to th coeld facts, we simply got to let on that a lantern’s resky. Whi, we cuud werk with a torchliet proseshun if we wonted to, I beleev. Now, whilst I think of it, we got to hunt up sumthing to maek a saw out of th ferst chans we get.”

“Whut do we wont of a saw?”

“Whut do we wont of it? Hain’t we got to saw th leg of Jim’s bed off, so as to get th chaen loos?”

“Whi, u just sed a body cuud lift up th bedsted and slip th chaen off.”

“Wel, if that ain’t just liek u, Huck Finn. U can get up th infant-schooliest ways of going at a thing. Whi, hain’t u ever red eny buuks at all?—Barron Trenck, nor Casanova, nor Benvenuto Chelleeny, nor Henri IV., nor nun of them heeroes? Hoo ever herd of geting a prizoner loos in such an oeld-maidy way as that? No; th way all th best authoritys duz is to saw th bed-leg in too, and leev it just so, and swolo th sawdust, so it can’t be found, and puut sum dert and grees around th sawd plaes so th verry keenest seneskal can’t see no sien of it’s being sawd, and thinks th bed-leg is perfectly sound. Then, th niet u’re redy, fech th leg a kik, doun she goes; slip off yur chaen, and thair u ar. Nuthing to do but hich yur roep lader to th batlments, shin doun it, braek yur leg in th moet—becauz a roep lader is nienteen fuut too short, u noe—and thair’s yur horses and yur trusty vassles, and thay scoop u up and fling u across a sadl, and away u go to yur naetiv Langudoc, or Navarre, or whairever it is. It’s gaudy, Huck. I wish thair was a moet to this cabin. If we get tiem, th niet of th escaep, we’ll dig wun.”

I sez:

“Whut do we wont of a moet when we’re going to snaek him out frum under th cabin?”

But he never herd me. He had forgot me and evrything els. He had his chin in his hand, thinking. Prity soon he sies and shaeks his hed; then sies agen, and sez:

“No, it wouldn’t do—thair ain’t nesesity enuf for it.”

“For whut?” I sez.

“Whi, to saw Jim’s leg off,” he sez.

“Guud land!” I sez; “whi, thair ain’t no nesesity for it. And whut wuud u wont to saw his leg off for, enyway?”











“Wel, sum of th best authoritys has dun it. Thay couldn’t get th chaen off, so thay just cut thair hand off and shuvd. And a leg wuud be beter stil. But we got to let that go. Thair ain’t nesesity enuf in this caes; and, besieds, Jim’s a nigger, and wouldn’t understand th reezons for it, and how it’s th custom in Europe; so we’ll let it go. But thair’s wun thing—he can hav a roep lader; we can teer up our sheets and maek him a roep lader eezy enuf. And we can send it to him in a pi; it’s moestly dun that way. And I’ve et wers pies.”

“Whi, Tom Sawyer, how u tauk,” I sez; “Jim ain’t got no ues for a roep lader.”

“He has got ues for it. How u tauk, u beter say; u don’t noe nuthing about it. He’s got to hav a roep lader; thay all do.”

“Whut in th naeshun can he do with it?”

“Do with it? He can hied it in his bed, can’t he?” That’s whut thay all do; and he’s got to, too. Huck, u don’t ever seem to wont to do enything that’s reguelar; u wont to be starting sumthing fresh all th tiem. S’poez he don’t do nuthing with it? ain’t it thair in his bed, for a cloo, after he’s gon? and don’t u rekon thay’ll wont clews? Of cors thay wil. And u wouldn’t leev them eny? That wuud be a prity houdy-do, wouldn’t it! I never herd of such a thing.”

“Wel,” I sez, “if it’s in th reguelaeshuns, and he’s got to hav it, all riet, let him hav it; becauz I don’t wish to go bak on no reguelaeshuns; but thair’s wun thing, Tom Sawyer—if we go to tairing up our sheets to maek Jim a roep lader, we’re going to get into trubl with Ant Sally, just as shur as u’re born. Now, th way I luuk at it, a hickry-bark lader don’t cost nuthing, and don’t waest nuthing, and is just as guud to loed up a pi with, and hied in a straw tik, as eny rag lader u can start; and as for Jim, he ain’t had no expeeryens, and so he don’t cair whut kiend of a—”

“O, shuks, Huck Finn, if I was as ignorant as u I’d keep stil—that’s whut I’D do. Hoo ever herd of a staet prizoner escaeping bi a hickry-bark lader? Whi, it’s perfectly ridicuelus.”

“Wel, all riet, Tom, fix it yur oen way; but if u’ll taek mi advies, u’ll let me borro a sheet off of th cloethslien.”

He sed that wuud do. And that gaev him anuther iedeea, and he sez:

“Borro a shert, too.”

“Whut do we wont of a shert, Tom?”

“Wont it for Jim to keep a jernal on.”

“Jernal yur grany—Jim can’t riet.”

“S’poez he can’t riet—he can maek marks on th shert, can’t he, if we maek him a pen out of an oeld pueter spoon or a pees of an oeld ieern barrel-hoop?”

“Whi, Tom, we can puul a fether out of a goos and maek him a beter wun; and qiker, too.”

“Prizoners don’t hav gees runing around th donjon-keep to puul pens out of, u muggins. Thay allways maek thair pens out of th hardest, tufest, troublesomest pees of oeld bras candlstik or sumthing liek that thay can get thair hands on; and it taeks them weeks and weeks and munths and munths to fiel it out, too, becauz thay’ve got to do it bi rubing it on th wall. Thay wouldn’t ues a goos-qil if thay had it. It ain’t reguelar.”

“Wel, then, whut’ll we maek him th ink out of?”

“Meny maeks it out of ieern-rust and teers; but that’s th comon sort and wimen; th best authoritys uezes thair oen blud. Jim can do that; and when he wonts to send eny litl comon ordinairy misteerius mesej to let th werld noe whair he’s captivaeted, he can riet it on th botom of a tin plaet with a fork and thro it out of th windo. Th Ieern Mask allways dun that, and it’s a blaem’ guud way, too.”

“Jim ain’t got no tin plates. Thay feed him in a pan.”

“That ain’t nuthing; we can get him sum.”

“Can’t noebody red his plates.”

“That ain’t got enything to do with it, Huck Finn. All he’s got to do is to riet on th plaet and thro it out. U don’t hav to be aebl to red it. Whi, haf th tiem u can’t red enything a prizoner riets on a tin plaet, or enywhair els.”

“Wel, then, whut’s th sens in waesting th plates?”

“Whi, blaem it all, it ain’t th prizoner’s plates.”

“But it’s sumbody’s plates, ain’t it?”

“Wel, spos’n it is? Whut duz th prizoner cair hoos—”

He broek off thair, becauz we herd th brekfast-horn bloeing. So we cleerd out for th hous.











Along during th morning I borroed a sheet and a whiet shert off of th cloeths-lien; and I found an oeld sak and puut them in it, and we went doun and got th fox-fier, and puut that in too. I calld it borroeing, becauz that was whut pap allways calld it; but Tom sed it worn’t borroeing, it was steeling. He sed we was reprezenting prizoners; and prizoners don’t cair how thay get a thing so thay get it, and noebody don’t blaem them for it, eether. It ain’t no criem in a prizoner to steel th thing he needs to get away with, Tom sed; it’s his riet; and so, as long as we was reprezenting a prizoner, we had a perfect riet to steel enything on this plaes we had th leest ues for to get ourselvs out of prizon with. He sed if we worn’t prizoners it wuud be a verry diferent thing, and noebody but a meen, ornery person wuud steel when he worn’t a prizoner. So we alowd we wuud steel evrything thair was that cum handy. And yet he maed a miety fus, wun day, after that, when I stoel a wautermelon out of th nigger-pach and eet it; and he maed me go and giv th niggers a diem without teling them whut it was for. Tom sed that whut he ment was, we cuud steel enything we needed. Wel, I sez, I needed th wautermelon. But he sed I didn’t need it to get out of prizon with; thair’s whair th diferens was. He sed if I’d a wonted it to hied a nief in, and smugl it to Jim to kil th seneskal with, it wuud a bin all riet. So I let it go at that, tho I couldn’t see no advantej in mi reprezenting a prizoner if I got to set doun and chaw oever a lot of goeld-leef distinkshuns liek that evry tiem I see a chans to hog a wautermelon.

Wel, as I was saying, we waeted that morning til evrybody was setld doun to biznes, and noebody in siet around th yard; then Tom he carryd th sak into th leen-to whilst I stuud off a pees to keep woch. Bi and bi he cum out, and we went and set doun on th wuudpiel to tauk. He sez:

“Evrything’s all riet now exsept tools; and that’s eezy fixt.”

“Tools?” I sez.

“Yes.”

“Tools for whut?”

“Whi, to dig with. We ain’t a-going to naw him out, ar we?”

“Ain’t them oeld cripld piks and things in thair guud enuf to dig a nigger out with?” I sez.

He terns on me, luuking pitying enuf to maek a body cri, and sez:

“Huck Finn, did u ever heer of a prizoner having piks and shuvels, and all th modern conveenyunses in his wordroeb to dig himself out with? Now I wont to ask u—if u got eny reasonableness in u at all—whut kiend of a sho wuud that giv him to be a heero? Whi, thay miet as wel lend him th kee and dun with it. Piks and shuvels—whi, thay wouldn’t fernish ’em to a king.”

“Wel, then,” I sez, “if we don’t wont th piks and shuvels, whut do we wont?”

“A cupl of caes-nievs.”

“To dig th foundaeshuns out frum under that cabin with?”

“Yes.”

“Confound it, it’s foolish, Tom.”

“It don’t maek no diferens how foolish it is, it’s th riet way—and it’s th reguelar way. And thair ain’t no uther way, that ever I herd of, and I’ve red all th buuks that givs eny informaeshun about thees things. Thay allways dig out with a caes-nief—and not thru dert, miend u; generly it’s thru solid rok. And it taeks them weeks and weeks and weeks, and for ever and ever. Whi, luuk at wun of them prizoners in th botom dunjon of th Casl Deef, in th harbor of Marseilles, that dug himself out that way; how long was he at it, u rekon?”

“I don’t noe.”

“Wel, ges.”

“I don’t noe. A munth and a haf.”

“Therty-seven yeer—and he cum out in Chiena. That’s th kiend. I wish th botom of this fortres was solid rok.”

“Jim don’t noe noebody in Chiena.”

“Whut’s that got to do with it? Neether did that uther felo. But u’re allways a-waandering off on a sied ishoo. Whi can’t u stik to th maen point?”

“All riet—I don’t cair whair he cums out, so he cums out; and Jim don’t, eether, I rekon. But thair’s wun thing, enyway—Jim’s too oeld to be dug out with a caes-nief. He wun’t last.”

“Yes he wil last, too. U don’t rekon it’s going to taek therty-seven yeers to dig out thru a dert foundaeshun, do u?”

“How long wil it taek, Tom?”

“Wel, we can’t resk being as long as we aut to, becauz it mayn’t taek verry long for Unkl Silas to heer frum doun thair bi Nue Orleans. He’ll heer Jim ain’t frum thair. Then his next moov wil be to advertiez Jim, or sumthing liek that. So we can’t resk being as long diging him out as we aut to. Bi riets I rekon we aut to be a cupl of yeers; but we can’t. Things being so unsertan, whut I recomend is this: that we reealy dig riet in, as qik as we can; and after that, we can let on, to ourselvs, that we was at it therty-seven yeers. Then we can snach him out and rush him away th ferst tiem thair’s an alarm. Yes, I rekon that ’ll be th best way.”

“Now, thair’s sens in that,” I sez. “Leting on don’t cost nuthing; leting on ain’t no trubl; and if it’s eny object, I don’t miend leting on we was at it a hundred and fifty yeer. It wouldn’t straen me nun, after I got mi hand in. So I’ll moezy along now, and smouch a cupl of caes-nievs.”











“Smouch three,” he sez; “we wont wun to maek a saw out of.”

“Tom, if it ain’t unregular and irrelijus to sejest it,” I sez, “thair’s an oeld rusty saw-blaed around yonder stiking under th wether-bording behiend th smoek-hous.”

He luukt kiend of weery and discurejd-liek, and sez:

“It ain’t no ues to tri to lern u nuthing, Huck. Run along and smouch th nievs—three of them.” So I dun it.
















CHAPTER XXXVI.

As soon as we rekond evrybody was asleep that niet we went doun th lietning-rod, and shut ourselvs up in th leen-to, and got out our piel of fox-fier, and went to werk. We cleerd evrything out of th way, about foer or fiev fuut along th midl of th botom log. Tom sed he was riet behiend Jim’s bed now, and we’d dig in under it, and when we got thru thair couldn’t noebody in th cabin ever noe thair was eny hoel thair, becauz Jim’s counter-pin hung doun moest to th ground, and u’d hav to raez it up and luuk under to see th hoel. So we dug and dug with th caes-nievs til moest midniet; and then we was daug-tierd, and our hands was blisterd, and yet u couldn’t see we’d dun enything hardly. At last I sez:

“This ain’t no therty-seven yeer job; this is a therty-aet yeer job, Tom Sawyer.”

He never sed nuthing. But he sied, and prity soon he stopt diging, and then for a guud litl whiel I knowed that he was thinking. Then he sez:

“It ain’t no ues, Huck, it ain’t a-going to werk. If we was prizoners it wuud, becauz then we’d hav as meny yeers as we wonted, and no hery; and we wouldn’t get but a fue minits to dig, evry day, whiel thay was chaenjing woches, and so our hands wouldn’t get blisterd, and we cuud keep it up riet along, yeer in and yeer out, and do it riet, and th way it aut to be dun. But we can’t fool along; we got to rush; we ain’t got no tiem to spair. If we was to puut in anuther niet this way we’d hav to nok off for a week to let our hands get wel—couldn’t tuch a caes-nief with them sooner.”

“Wel, then, whut we going to do, Tom?”

“I’ll tel u. It ain’t riet, and it ain’t moral, and I wouldn’t liek it to get out; but thair ain’t oenly just th wun way: we got to dig him out with th piks, and let on it’s caes-nievs.”

“Now u’re tauking!” I sez; “yur hed gets leveler and leveler all th tiem, Tom Sawyer,” I sez. “Piks is th thing, moral or no moral; and as for me, I don’t cair shuks for th morality of it, nohow. When I start in to steel a nigger, or a wautermelon, or a Sunday-scool buuk, I ain’t no ways particuelar how it’s dun so it’s dun. Whut I wont is mi nigger; or whut I wont is mi wautermelon; or whut I wont is mi Sunday-scool buuk; and if a pik’s th handyest thing, that’s th thing I’m a-going to dig that nigger or that wautermelon or that Sunday-scool buuk out with; and I don’t giv a ded rat whut th authoritys thinks about it nuther.”

“Wel,” he sez, “thair’s excues for piks and leting-on in a caes liek this; if it worn’t so, I wouldn’t aproov of it, nor I wouldn’t stand bi and see th rools broek—becauz riet is riet, and rong is rong, and a body ain’t got no biznes doing rong when he ain’t ignorant and noes beter. It miet anser for u to dig Jim out with a pik, without eny leting on, becauz u don’t noe no beter; but it wouldn’t for me, becauz I do noe beter. Gimme a caes-nief.”

He had his oen bi him, but I handed him mien. He flung it doun, and sez:

“Gimme a caes-nief.”

I didn’t noe just whut to do—but then I thaut. I scracht around amungst th oeld tools, and got a pikax and giv it to him, and he tuuk it and went to werk, and never sed a werd.

He was allways just that particuelar. Fuul of prinsipl.

So then I got a shuvel, and then we pikt and shuveld, tern about, and maed th fer fli. We stuk to it about a haf an our, which was as long as we cuud stand up; but we had a guud deel of a hoel to sho for it. When I got up stairs I luukt out at th windo and see Tom doing his level best with th lietning-rod, but he couldn’t cum it, his hands was so sor. At last he sez:

“It ain’t no ues, it can’t be dun. Whut u rekon I beter do? Can’t u think of no way?”

“Yes,” I sez, “but I rekon it ain’t reguelar. Cum up th stairs, and let on it’s a lietning-rod.”

So he dun it.











Next day Tom stoel a pueter spoon and a bras candlstik in th hous, for to maek sum pens for Jim out of, and six talo candls; and I hung around th nigger cabins and laed for a chans, and stoel three tin plates. Tom sez it wasn’t enuf; but I sed noebody wouldn’t ever see th plates that Jim throwed out, becauz thay’d fall in th daug-fenel and jimpson weeds under th windo-hoel—then we cuud toet them bak and he cuud ues them oever agen. So Tom was satisfied. Then he sez:

“Now, th thing to study out is, how to get th things to Jim.”

“Taek them in thru th hoel,” I sez, “when we get it dun.”

He oenly just luukt scornful, and sed sumthing about noebody ever herd of such an idiotic iedeea, and then he went to studying. Bi and bi he sed he had ciphered out too or three ways, but thair worn’t no need to desied on eny of them yet. Sed we’d got to poest Jim ferst.

That niet we went doun th lietning-rod a litl after ten, and tuuk wun of th candls along, and lisend under th windo-hoel, and herd Jim snoring; so we picht it in, and it didn’t waek him. Then we wherld in with th pik and shuvel, and in about too ours and a haf th job was dun. We crept in under Jim’s bed and into th cabin, and pawed around and found th candl and lit it, and stuud oever Jim awhiel, and found him luuking harty and helthy, and then we woek him up jentl and grajual. He was so glad to see us he moest cried; and calld us huny, and all th pet naems he cuud think of; and was for having us hunt up a coeld-chizel to cut th chaen off of his leg with riet away, and cleering out without loozing eny tiem. But Tom he shoed him how unregular it wuud be, and set doun and toeld him all about our plans, and how we cuud allter them in a minit eny tiem thair was an alarm; and not to be th leest afraed, becauz we wuud see he got away, shur. So Jim he sed it was all riet, and we set thair and taukt oever oeld tiems awhiel, and then Tom askt a lot of qeschuns, and when Jim toeld him Unkl Silas cum in evry day or too to pray with him, and Ant Sally cum in to see if he was cumfortabl and had plenty to eet, and boeth of them was kiend as thay cuud be, Tom sez:

“Now I noe how to fix it. We’ll send u sum things bi them.”

I sed, “Don’t do nuthing of th kiend; it’s wun of th moest jakas iedeeas I ever struk;” but he never paed no atenshun to me; went riet on. It was his way when he’d got his plans set.

So he toeld Jim how we’d hav to smugl in th roep-lader pi and uther larj things bi Nat, th nigger that fed him, and he must be on th luukout, and not be serpriezd, and not let Nat see him oepen them; and we wuud puut small things in unkl’s coet-pokets and he must steel them out; and we wuud tee things to ant’s aepron-strings or puut them in her aepron-poket, if we got a chans; and toeld him whut thay wuud be and whut thay was for. And toeld him how to keep a jernal on th shert with his blud, and all that. He toeld him evrything. Jim he couldn’t see no sens in th moest of it, but he alowd we was whiet foeks and knowed beter than him; so he was satisfied, and sed he wuud do it all just as Tom sed.

Jim had plenty corn-cob pieps and tobaco; so we had a riet doun guud soeshabl tiem; then we cralld out thru th hoel, and so hoem to bed, with hands that luukt liek thay’d bin chawed. Tom was in hi spirits. He sed it was th best fun he ever had in his lief, and th moest intellectural; and sed if he oenly cuud see his way to it we wuud keep it up all th rest of our lievs and leev Jim to our children to get out; for he beleevd Jim wuud cum to liek it beter and beter th mor he got uezd to it. He sed that in that way it cuud be strung out to as much as aety yeer, and wuud be th best tiem on record. And he sed it wuud maek us all selebraeted that had a hand in it.

In th morning we went out to th wuudpiel and chopt up th bras candlstik into handy siezes, and Tom puut them and th pueter spoon in his poket. Then we went to th nigger cabins, and whiel I got Nat’s noetis off, Tom shuvd a pees of candlstik into th midl of a corn-pone that was in Jim’s pan, and we went along with Nat to see how it wuud werk, and it just werkt noebl; when Jim bit into it it moest masht all his teeth out; and thair worn’t ever enything cuud a werkt beter. Tom sed so himself. Jim he never let on but whut it was oenly just a pees of rok or sumthing liek that that’s allways geting into bred, u noe; but after that he never bit into nuthing but whut he jabd his fork into it in three or foer plaeses ferst.

And whilst we was a-standing thair in th dimmish liet, heer cums a cupl of th hounds buljing in frum under Jim’s bed; and thay kept on pieling in til thair was eleven of them, and thair worn’t hardly room in thair to get yur breth. Bi jings, we forgot to fasen that leen-to dor! Th nigger Nat he oenly just holerd “Wiches” wuns, and keeled oever on to th flor amungst th daugs, and begun to groen liek he was dieing. Tom jerkt th dor oepen and flung out a slab of Jim’s meet, and th daugs went for it, and in too seconds he was out himself and bak agen and shut th dor, and I knowed he’d fixt th uther dor too. Then he went to werk on th nigger, coexing him and peting him, and asking him if he’d bin imajining he saw sumthing agen. He raezd up, and blinkt his ies around, and sez:

“Mars Sid, u’ll say I’s a fool, but if I didn’t b’lieve I see moest a milyon daugs, er devils, er sum’n, I wisht I may die riet heah in dese traks. I did, mos’ sholy. Mars Sid, I felt um—I felt um, sah; day was all oever me. Dad fech it, I jis’ wisht I cuud git mi han’s on wun er dem wiches jis’ wunst—on’y jis’ wunst—it’s all I’d ast. But mos’ly I wisht day’d lemme ’loen, I duz.”

Tom sez:

“Wel, I tel u whut I think. Whut maeks them cum heer just at this runaway nigger’s brekfast-tiem? It’s becauz thay’re hunggry; that’s th reezon. U maek them a wich pi; that’s th thing for u to do.”











“But mi lan’, Mars Sid, how’s I gwyne to maek ’m a wich pi? I doan’ noe how to maek it. I hain’t ever hearn er sich a thing b’fo’.”

“Wel, then, I’ll hav to maek it mieself.”

“Wil u do it, huny?—wil u? I’ll wusshup de groun’ und’ yo’ fuut, I wil!”

“All riet, I’ll do it, seeing it’s u, and u’ve bin guud to us and shoed us th runaway nigger. But u got to be miety cairful. When we cum around, u tern yur bak; and then whutever we’ve puut in th pan, don’t u let on u see it at all. And don’t u luuk when Jim unloeds th pan—sumthing miet hapen, I don’t noe whut. And abuv all, don’t u handl th wich-things.”

“Hannel ‘M, Mars Sid? Whut is u a-talkin’ ’bout? I wouldn’ lay de waet er mi fingger on um, not f’r ten hund’d thous’n bilyon dolars, I wouldn’t.”














CHAPTER XXXVII.

That was all fixt. So then we went away and went to th rubbage-piel in th bak yard, whair thay keep th oeld boots, and rags, and peeses of botls, and wor-out tin things, and all such truk, and scracht around and found an oeld tin washpan, and stopt up th hoels as wel as we cuud, to baek th pi in, and tuuk it doun selar and stoel it fuul of flour and started for brekfast, and found a cupl of shinggl-naels that Tom sed wuud be handy for a prizoner to scrabble his naem and sorroes on th dunjon walls with, and dropt wun of them in Ant Sally’s aepron-poket which was hanging on a chair, and t’uther we stuk in th band of Unkl Silas’s hat, which was on th buero, becauz we herd th children say thair paa and maa was going to th runaway nigger’s hous this morning, and then went to brekfast, and Tom dropt th pueter spoon in Unkl Silas’s coet-poket, and Ant Sally wasn’t cum yet, so we had to waet a litl whiel.

And when she cum she was hot and red and cross, and couldn’t hardly waet for th blesing; and then she went to sloosing out coffy with wun hand and craking th handyest chield’s hed with her thimbl with th uther, and sez:

“I’ve hunted hi and I’ve hunted lo, and it duz beet all whut has becum of yur uther shert.”

Mi hart fel doun amungst mi lungs and livers and things, and a hard pees of corn-crust started doun mi throet after it and got met on th roed with a cauf, and was shot across th taebl, and tuuk wun of th children in th ie and curld him up liek a fishing-werm, and let a cri out of him th siez of a warwhoop, and Tom he ternd kiender bloo around th gils, and it all amounted to a considerabl staet of things for about a qorter of a minit or as much as that, and I wuud a soeld out for haf pries if thair was a bider. But after that we was all riet agen—it was th suden serpriez of it that nokt us so kiend of coeld. Unkl Silas he sez:

“It’s moest uncomon cuerius, I can’t understand it. I noe perfectly wel I tuuk it off, becauz—”

“Becauz u hain’t got but wun on. Just lisen at th man! I noe u tuuk it off, and noe it bi a beter way than yur wuul-gethering memory, too, becauz it was on th clo’s-lien yesterday—I see it thair mieself. But it’s gon, that’s th long and th short of it, and u’ll just hav to chaenj to a red flann’l wun til I can get tiem to maek a nue wun. And it ’ll be th therd I’ve maed in too yeers. It just keeps a body on th jump to keep u in sherts; and whutever u do manej to do with ’m all is mor’n I can maek out. A body ’d think u wuud lern to taek sum sort of cair of ’em at yur tiem of lief.”

“I noe it, Sally, and I do tri all I can. But it oughtn’t to be alltogether mi fallt, becauz, u noe, I don’t see them nor hav nuthing to do with them exsept when thay’re on me; and I don’t beleev I’ve ever lost wun of them off of me.”

“Wel, it ain’t yur fallt if u haeven’t, Silas; u’d a dun it if u cuud, I rekon. And th shert ain’t all that’s gon, nuther. Ther’s a spoon gon; and that ain’t all. Thair was ten, and now ther’s oenly nien. Th caf got th shert, I rekon, but th caf never tuuk th spoon, that’s serten.”

“Whi, whut els is gon, Sally?”

“Ther’s six candls gon—that’s whut. Th rats cuud a got th candls, and I rekon thay did; I wunder thay don’t wauk off with th hoel plaes, th way u’re allways going to stop thair hoels and don’t do it; and if thay worn’t fools thay’d sleep in yur hair, Silas—u’d never fiend it out; but u can’t lay th spoon on th rats, and that I noe.”

“Wel, Sally, I’m in fallt, and I aknolej it; I’ve bin remiss; but I wun’t let to-morro go bi without stoping up them hoels.”

“O, I wouldn’t hery; next yeer ’ll do. Matilda Angelina Araminta Phelps!”

Whak cums th thimbl, and th chield snaches her claws out of th shuugar-boel without fooling around eny. Just then th nigger wuuman steps on to th pasej, and sez:

“Missus, day’s a sheet gon.”











“A sheet gon! Wel, for th land’s saek!”

“I’ll stop up them hoels to-day,” sez Unkl Silas, luuking sorroeful.

“O, do shet up!—s’poez th rats tuuk th sheet? whair’s it gon, Lize?”

“Clah to guudnes I hain’t no noeshun, Mis’ Sally. She wuz on de clo’sline yistiddy, but she dun gon: she ain’ dah no mo’ now.”

“I rekon th werld is cuming to an end. I never see th beet of it in all mi born days. A shert, and a sheet, and a spoon, and six can—”

“Missus,” cums a yung yaller wench, “day’s a bras cannelstick mis’n.”

“Cler out frum heer, u husy, er I’ll taek a skilet to ye!”

Wel, she was just a-biling. I begun to lay for a chans; I rekond I wuud sneek out and go for th wuuds til th wether moderated. She kept a-raejing riet along, runing her inserecshun all bi herself, and evrybody els miety meek and qieet; and at last Unkl Silas, luuking kiend of foolish, fishes up that spoon out of his poket. She stopt, with her mouth oepen and her hands up; and as for me, I wisht I was in Jeruslem or somewheres. But not long, becauz she sez:

“It’s just as I expected. So u had it in yur poket all th tiem; and liek as not u’ve got th uther things thair, too. How’d it get thair?”

“I reely don’t noe, Sally,” he sez, kiend of apolojiezing, “or u noe I wuud tel. I was a-studying oever mi text in Acts Seventeen befor brekfast, and I rekon I puut it in thair, not noetising, meening to puut mi Testament in, and it must be so, becauz mi Testament ain’t in; but I’ll go and see; and if th Testament is whair I had it, I’ll noe I didn’t puut it in, and that wil sho that I laed th Testament doun and tuuk up th spoon, and—”

“O, for th land’s saek! Giv a body a rest! Go ’long now, th hoel kit and biling of ye; and don’t cum ni me agen til I’ve got bak mi pees of miend.”

I’D a herd her if she’d a sed it to herself, let aloen speeking it out; and I’d a got up and oebayd her if I’d a bin ded. As we was pasing thru th seting-room th oeld man he tuuk up his hat, and th shinggl-nael fel out on th flor, and he just meerly pikt it up and laed it on th mantel-shelf, and never sed nuthing, and went out. Tom see him do it, and rememberd about th spoon, and sez:

“Wel, it ain’t no ues to send things bi him no mor, he ain’t relieabl.” Then he sez: “But he dun us a guud tern with th spoon, enyway, without noeing it, and so we’ll go and do him wun without him noeing it—stop up his rat-hoels.”

Thair was a noebl guud lot of them doun selar, and it tuuk us a hoel our, but we dun th job tiet and guud and shipshaep. Then we herd steps on th stairs, and blowed out our liet and hid; and heer cums th oeld man, with a candl in wun hand and a bundl of stuf in t’uther, luuking as absent-miended as yeer befor last. He went a mooning around, ferst to wun rat-hoel and then anuther, til he’d bin to them all. Then he stuud about fiev minits, piking talo-drip off of his candl and thinking. Then he terns off slo and dreemy tords th stairs, saying:

“Wel, for th lief of me I can’t remember when I dun it. I cuud sho her now that I worn’t to blaem on acount of th rats. But never miend—let it go. I rekon it wouldn’t do no guud.”

And so he went on a-mumbling up stairs, and then we left. He was a miety nies oeld man. And allways is.

Tom was a guud deel botherd about whut to do for a spoon, but he sed we’d got to hav it; so he tuuk a think. When he had ciphered it out he toeld me how we was to do; then we went and waeted around th spoon-basket til we see Ant Sally cuming, and then Tom went to counting th spoons and laying them out to wun sied, and I slid wun of them up mi sleev, and Tom sez:

“Whi, Ant Sally, thair ain’t but nien spoons yet.”

She sez:

“Go ’long to yur play, and don’t bother me. I noe beter, I counted ’m mieself.”

“Wel, I’ve counted them twies, Aunty, and I can’t maek but nien.”

She luukt out of all paeshens, but of cors she cum to count—enybody wuud.

“I declair to graeshus ther’ ain’t but nien!” she sez. “Whi, whut in th werld—plaeg taek th things, I’ll count ’m agen.”

So I slipt bak th wun I had, and when she got dun counting, she sez:

“Hang th trublsum rubbage, ther’s ten now!” and she luukt hufy and botherd boeth. But Tom sez:

“Whi, Aunty, I don’t think thair’s ten.”

“U numskul, didn’t u see me count ’m?”

“I noe, but—”

“Wel, I’ll count ’m agen.”











So I smouched wun, and thay cum out nien, saem as th uther tiem. Wel, she was in a tairing way—just a-trembling all oever, she was so mad. But she counted and counted til she got that addled she’d start to count in th basket for a spoon sumtiems; and so, three tiems thay cum out riet, and three tiems thay cum out rong. Then she grabd up th basket and slamd it across th hous and nokt th cat galy-west; and she sed cle’r out and let her hav sum pees, and if we cum bothering around her agen betwixt that and diner she’d skin us. So we had th od spoon, and dropt it in her aepron-poket whilst she was a-giving us our saeling orders, and Jim got it all riet, along with her shinggl nael, befor noon. We was verry wel satisfied with this biznes, and Tom alowd it was werth twies th trubl it tuuk, becauz he sed now she couldn’t ever count them spoons twies aliek agen to saev her lief; and wouldn’t beleev she’d counted them riet if she did; and sed that after she’d about counted her hed off for th next three days he jujd she’d giv it up and offer to kil enybody that wonted her to ever count them eny mor.

So we puut th sheet bak on th lien that niet, and stoel wun out of her clozet; and kept on puuting it bak and steeling it agen for a cupl of days til she didn’t noe how meny sheets she had eny mor, and she didn’t cair, and worn’t a-going to buulyrag th rest of her soel out about it, and wouldn’t count them agen not to saev her lief; she druther die ferst.

So we was all riet now, as to th shert and th sheet and th spoon and th candls, bi th help of th caf and th rats and th mixt-up counting; and as to th candlstik, it worn’t no conseqens, it wuud blo oever bi and bi.

But that pi was a job; we had no end of trubl with that pi. We fixt it up away doun in th wuuds, and cuukt it thair; and we got it dun at last, and verry satisfactory, too; but not all in wun day; and we had to ues up three wosh-pans fuul of flour befor we got thru, and we got bernt prity much all oever, in plaeses, and ies puut out with th smoek; becauz, u see, we didn’t wont nuthing but a crust, and we couldn’t prop it up riet, and she wuud allways caev in. But of cors we thaut of th riet way at last—which was to cuuk th lader, too, in th pi. So then we laed in with Jim th second niet, and tore up th sheet all in litl strings and twisted them together, and long befor dayliet we had a luvly roep that u cuud a hung a person with. We let on it tuuk nien munths to maek it.

And in th fornoon we tuuk it doun to th wuuds, but it wouldn’t go into th pi. Being maed of a hoel sheet, that way, thair was roep enuf for forty pies if we’d a wonted them, and plenty left oever for soop, or sausej, or enything u chooz. We cuud a had a hoel diner.











But we didn’t need it. All we needed was just enuf for th pi, and so we throwed th rest away. We didn’t cuuk nun of th pies in th wosh-pan—afraed th soder wuud melt; but Unkl Silas he had a noebl bras worming-pan which he thaut considerabl of, becauz it belongd to wun of his ancesters with a long wuuden handl that cum oever frum England with William th Conkeror in th Mayflower or wun of them erly ships and was hid away up garret with a lot of uther oeld pots and things that was valueabl, not on acount of being eny acount, becauz thay worn’t, but on acount of them being relicts, u noe, and we snaekt her out, prievet, and tuuk her doun thair, but she faeld on th ferst pies, becauz we didn’t noe how, but she cum up smieling on th last wun. We tuuk and liend her with doe, and set her in th coels, and loeded her up with rag roep, and puut on a doe roof, and shut doun th lid, and puut hot embers on top, and stuud off fiev fuut, with th long handl, cool and cumfortabl, and in fifteen minits she ternd out a pi that was a satisfacshun to luuk at. But th person that et it wuud wont to fech a cupl of kags of toothpiks along, for if that roep lader wouldn’t cramp him doun to biznes I don’t noe nuthing whut I’m tauking about, and lay him in enuf stumac-aek to last him til next tiem, too.

Nat didn’t luuk when we puut th wich pi in Jim’s pan; and we puut th three tin plates in th botom of th pan under th vittles; and so Jim got evrything all riet, and as soon as he was bi himself he busted into th pi and hid th roep lader insied of his straw tik, and scracht sum marks on a tin plaet and throwed it out of th windo-hoel.














CHAPTER XXXVIII.

Maeking them pens was a distressid tuf job, and so was th saw; and Jim alowd th inscripshun was going to be th tufest of all. That’s th wun which th prizoner has to scrabble on th wall. But he had to hav it; Tom sed he’d got to; thair worn’t no caes of a staet prizoner not scrabbling his inscripshun to leev behiend, and his coet of arms.

“Luuk at Laedy Jane Gray,” he sez; “luuk at Gilford Dudley; luuk at oeld Northumberland! Whi, Huck, s’poez it is considerble trubl?—whut u going to do?—how u going to get around it? Jim’s got to do his inscripshun and coet of arms. Thay all do.”

Jim sez:

“Whi, Mars Tom, I hain’t got no coet o’ arm; I hain’t got nuffn but dish yer oelay shert, en u noes I got to keep de jernal on dat.”

“O, u don’t understand, Jim; a coet of arms is verry diferent.”

“Wel,” I sez, “Jim’s riet, enyway, when he sez he ain’t got no coet of arms, becauz he hain’t.”

“I rekon I knowed that,” Tom sez, “but u bet he’ll hav wun befor he goes out of this—becauz he’s going out riet, and thair ain’t going to be no flaws in his record.”

So whilst me and Jim field away at th pens on a brikbat apees, Jim a-maeking his’n out of th bras and I maeking mien out of th spoon, Tom set to werk to think out th coet of arms. Bi and bi he sed he’d struk so meny guud wuns he didn’t hardly noe which to taek, but thair was wun which he rekond he’d desied on. He sez:

“On th scutcheon we’ll hav a bend or in th dexter baes, a saltire murrey in th fes, with a daug, couchant, for comon charj, and under his fuut a chaen embatld, for slaevery, with a shevron vert in a cheef engrailed, and three invected liens on a feeld azher, with th nombril points rampant on a dancette indented; crest, a runaway nigger, saebl, with his bundl oever his shoelder on a bar sinister; and a cupl of gules for suporters, which is u and me; moto, Maggiore Fretta, Minore Otto. Got it out of a buuk—meens th mor haest th les speed.”

“Geewhillikins,” I sez, “but whut duz th rest of it meen?”

“We ain’t got no tiem to bother oever that,” he sez; “we got to dig in liek all git-out.”

“Wel, enyway,” I sez, “whut’s sum of it? Whut’s a fes?”

“A fes—a fes is—u don’t need to noe whut a fes is. I’ll sho him how to maek it when he gets to it.”

“Shuks, Tom,” I sez, “I think u miet tel a person. Whut’s a bar sinister?”

“O, I don’t noe. But he’s got to hav it. All th noebility duz.”

That was just his way. If it didn’t soot him to explaen a thing to u, he wouldn’t do it. U miet pump at him a week, it wouldn’t maek no diferens.

He’d got all that coet of arms biznes fixt, so now he started in to finish up th rest of that part of th werk, which was to plan out a mornful inscripshun—sed Jim got to hav wun, liek thay all dun. He maed up a lot, and roet them out on a paeper, and red them off, so:

1. Heer a captiv hart busted. 2. Heer a pur prizoner, forsuuk bi th werld and frends, freted his sorroeful lief. 3. Heer a loenly hart broek, and a worn spirit went to its rest, after therty-seven yeers of solitairy captivity. 4. Heer, hoemles and frendles, after therty-seven yeers of biter captivity, perrisht a noebl straenjer, nacheral sun of Louis XIV.

Tom’s vois trembld whilst he was reeding them, and he moest broek doun. When he got dun he couldn’t no way maek up his miend which wun for Jim to scrabble on to th wall, thay was all so guud; but at last he alowd he wuud let him scrabble them all on. Jim sed it wuud taek him a yeer to scrabble such a lot of truk on to th logs with a nael, and he didn’t noe how to maek leters, besieds; but Tom sed he wuud blok them out for him, and then he wouldn’t hav nuthing to do but just folo th liens. Then prity soon he sez:

“Cum to think, th logs ain’t a-going to do; thay don’t hav log walls in a dunjon: we got to dig th inscripshuns into a rok. We’ll fech a rok.”

Jim sed th rok was wers than th logs; he sed it wuud taek him such a pison long tiem to dig them into a rok he wouldn’t ever get out. But Tom sed he wuud let me help him do it. Then he tuuk a luuk to see how me and Jim was geting along with th pens. It was moest pesky teedius hard werk and slo, and didn’t giv mi hands no sho to get wel of th sors, and we didn’t seem to maek no hedway, hardly; so Tom sez:

“I noe how to fix it. We got to hav a rok for th coet of arms and mornful inscripshuns, and we can kil too berds with that saem rok. Thair’s a gaudy big griendstoen doun at th mil, and we’ll smouch it, and carv th things on it, and fiel out th pens and th saw on it, too.”











It worn’t no slouch of an iedeea; and it worn’t no slouch of a griendstoen nuther; but we alowd we’d takl it. It worn’t qiet midniet yet, so we cleerd out for th mil, leeving Jim at werk. We smouched th griendstoen, and set out to roel her hoem, but it was a moest naeshun tuf job. Sumtiems, do whut we cuud, we couldn’t keep her frum falling oever, and she cum miety neer mashing us evry tiem. Tom sed she was going to get wun of us, shur, befor we got thru. We got her haf way; and then we was plum playd out, and moest drownded with swet. We see it worn’t no ues; we got to go and fech Jim. So he raezd up his bed and slid th chaen off of th bed-leg, and wrapt it round and round his nek, and we cralld out thru our hoel and doun thair, and Jim and me laed into that griendstoen and waukt her along liek nuthing; and Tom superintended. He cuud out-sooperintend eny boy I ever see. He knowed how to do evrything.

Our hoel was prity big, but it worn’t big enuf to get th griendstoen thru; but Jim he tuuk th pik and soon maed it big enuf.
